Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 1049-1222 CE ♥ Airlangga's reign ended in 1049, after which point the kingdom was divided between his two sons.[2] The Singhasari Kingdom began in 1222.[3]

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ nominal ♥ A political structure evolved that despite being decentralised, allowed a flow of income to the central government. [4]. Although this supported a sophisticated level of court life, there was still no centre of power and instead a number of principalities and vassal states vied for dominance. [5]

♠ Language ♣ Sanskrit; Old Javanese ♥ Kediri = 'Indian Mulberry' in Sanskrit. ([2]) The translations of Hindu epic Ramayana and Mahabharata into old Javanese language took place during the era of Medang Kingdom and Kediri kingdom around 9th to 11th century. ([3])

♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European; Austronesian ♥ Sanskrit = Indo-European, Javanese = Austronesian ([4])

Map[edit]

[7]

General Description[edit]

The kingdom of Kediri ruled over a territory roughly corresponding to the modern-day province of East Java, at least based on the locations where its rulers' inscriptions have been found.[8] Midway through the eleventh century CE, the Javanese monarch Airlangga split his realm into two smaller kingdoms, each to be ruled by one of his sons: one of these kingdoms was likely Kediri[9]. The latter rapidly overshadowed its "twin": it was the first Indonesian kingdom to develop stratified territorial administration, it became known for the organization of its armed forces, and it even introduced bureaucratic officials specifically tasked with the management of the polity's water resources.[10] Kediri does not appear to have collapsed: rather, in the early thirteenth century, the cente of power shifted to Singhasari, with the foundation of a new dynasty.[11]

Population and political organization

Kediri was ruled by a king, who was assisted by bureaucratic officials. Moreover, as already mentioned, Kediri was the first known Indonesian state to develop stratified territorial administration: more specifically, the polity was organized into three levels, the village, the cluster of villages, and the state.[12] This suggests that there were officials in charge of administrating both village clusters and individual villages.

According to McEvedy and Jones,[13] Indonesia had a population of four million in 1100 CE. Given the fact that Kediri occupied a small portion of the archipelago, it seems reasonable to infer a population size of a few hundred thousands.

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [50,000-70,000] ♥ in squared kilometers.

"Kadiri was an ancient kingdom on the island of Java." [14]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [200,000-300,000]: 1100 CE; [250,000-350,000]: 1200 CE ♥ People.

Estimates for Indonesia (less West New Guinea): 4.0m in 1100 CE. 4.5m in 1200 CE[15]

Total area of Indonesia (less West New Guinea) 1,500,000 km2.[16]

On the basis of territorial extent, Kediri, which was 50,000-70,000 km2 may have had 3-5% of the Indonesian population. 200,000 in 1100 CE, 225,000 in 120,000 CE. Will use these figures for the bottom end of a range that assumes this well-organized polity was more density populated than surrounding regions.

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ 3 ♥ levels.

1. Large town e.g. capital Kediri/Daha

2. Village
3. Hamlet

Ruling class, religious authority, hamlets, non-farming sub-communities, commoners, slaves. Hamlets within villages came to increased prominence and became taxable units within the larger community. Other non-farming sub-communities emerged as regular features of expanded settlement complexes e.g. groups of artisans, small religious establishments, and merchant enclaves.[17]

" [18]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ [4-5] ♥ levels.

Increasingly hierarchical state administrative structure. By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command passed from the monarch through 'expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority'. [19]


1. King

"The capital, where the king abided in his palace, was called nagara, and the palace itself was called kadatwan. The denomination of the capital may, occasionally, be used as a synonym for the whole country. Based on the location where inscriptions issued by authorities of the Kadiri kingdom have been found, the territory of this kingdom can be identified as within the present-day East Java Province." [20]

_Central government_

2. upper level bureaucrats functioning as intermediaries
3. mid level bureaucrats who needed to go through upper bureaucrats for favours
4. Storehouse manager for rice
5. Storehouse worker
village officials managed irrigation system and guaranteed supply of rice to capital in exchange for privileges.[21]

_Provincial government_

2. Several villages (wisaya)
"It was the first kingdom known in Indonesia to have developed a stratified territorial administration, consisting of three levels: the village (called thani, which itself consisted of several subdivisions, each having its own name); the coordinated unity, made up of several villages (called wisaya); and the state or kingdom (called bhumi)." [22]
3. Village (thani)
4. officials at central village level
both of which managed irrigation system and guaranteed supply of rice to capital in exchange for privileges.[23]
4. Subdivision of village


♠ Religious levels ♣ 3 ♥ levels. No firm data, but inferred on the basis of Majapahit. 1) Buddhist and Saivite clergy (sogata); or 1) Vaisnava clergy (wipra); 2) local religious specialists (resi); 3) residential communities of monks (caturdwija); and 3) rural shamans (jangga). [24]

♠ Military levels ♣ 5 ♥ levels.

1. King

2. General inferred
3. Officer of higher group?
4. Unit leader
"Every unit had a leader and a certain symbol put on its flag. Most symbols used a figure of an animal."[25]
5. Individual soldier

"Aside from territorial administration, the Kadiri period also saw developments in military organization and mobilization. Troops were divided according to the mastery of different kinds of weapons, such as the club, the arrow, the battle-ax, and the lance, or according to mastery over different serving animals, such as the elephant and the horse."[26]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥ Law codified by Airlangga who drew together local traditions, however this is not extant. It is unclear when this codified law fell out of use. [31]

♠ Judges ♣ ♥ unknown

♠ Courts ♣ inferred present ♥ Stepped roof type pendopo once sheltered the institutions of ancient Javanese kingdoms, such as law courts, clergy, palaces, and for public appearances of the king and his ministers. [32]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥

♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥

♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ Kediri pioneered a system of water management for both transportation and irrigation. [33]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ inferred absent ♥ no mention of drinking water projects: "Initiatives in the Kadiri state formation also including the development of water management (supposedly for both transportation and irrigation purposes). A special government official appointed for this task, the senapati sarwwajala, first appeared during the Kadiri period. A water-related professional that was first mentioned in Kadiri inscriptions was the undahagi lancang, the shipbuilder. Another official that likewise first appeared during the Kadiri period was the sopana, who acted as an intermediary between the king and those who needed the king's favor." [34]
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ Markets and the trades associated with them are frequently mentioned in the legal literature. These markets circulated among groups of neighbouring communities at the village rather than hamlet level.[35]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥ Sedyawati refers to granaries in the rajya, the 'city of the king'.[36]

♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ ♥ unknown

♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ ♥ associated with government and temples

Military use of Metals[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ present ♥ Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [53]
♠ Bronze ♣ present ♥ “Bronze metallurgy was practiced in at least Southern Vietnam, the islands surrounding the Sulu and Sulawesi seas, West Malaysia, South Sumatra, and especially Java and Bali." [54] Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [55]
♠ Iron ♣ present ♥ “Iron tools, best evidenced in Southern Vietnam, West Malaysia, and Java, were attached to handles (presum- ably wooden) via tangs, sockets, or shaft holes."[56] Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [57]
♠ Steel ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Historical records show "good quality Indian steel" was reaching Ethiopia in 200 BCE[58] - did they also export across the Bay of Bengal? Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [59]


Projectiles[edit]

♠ Javelins ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No mention of thrown spears. Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[60] The switch-over did not occur until the end of the Kediri Kingdom: it was the Singhasari Kingdom that witnessed 'the decline of Hindu culture and civilisation in Java and the succession of Javanese culture.'[61] Temple reliefs from earlier periods contain murals showing clubs, swords, bows and arrows, spears, shields, armour, knives, halberds.[62] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese: "war, weapon, sword, lance, armour, shield, helmet, banner, battle, siege, fortress, soldier, officer, enemy, spy, etc."[63]

Naval technology[edit]

♠ Small vessels (canoes, etc) ♣ present ♥ central government controlled building and use of boats. [148]
♠ Merchant ships pressed into service ♣ ♥
♠ Specialized military vessels ♣ inferred present ♥ Professional seamen. [149] "A water-related professional that was first mentioned in Kadiri inscriptions was the undahagi lancang, the shipbuilder."[150] According to the Chinese the Kediri kingdom was an even greater maritime power than the Sailendra empire, controlled islands such as Bali, Borneo, South-Celebes.[151]

Fortifications[edit]

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ inferred absent ♥ Convenience and pressures to reduce social tensions appear to have over-ridden considerations of defence in the location of housing from the early tenth century. [152]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ present ♥ 'The people make fortifications of wood...' [153] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[154]
♠ Earth ramparts ♣ inferred present ♥ Reference suggested a code of 'present' for Medang period.[155] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[156] "'In this country they have made the city walls of piled-up bricks, the wall has double gates and watch-towers,' wrote a Chinese voyager who went to Java fourteen centuries ago."[157]
♠ Ditch ♣ inferred present ♥ Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[158] In Medang period Ratu Boko had a dry moat as a defensive structure [159]
♠ Moat ♣ inferred present ♥ Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[160] In Medang period Ratu Boko had a dry moat as a defensive structure [161]
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Ratu Boko had stone walls as defensive structure. [162] Borobudur stone laid without mortar - this was a temple. ([6])
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ In Medang period Ratu Boko had stone walls as defensive structure.[163] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[164]

Other technologies[edit]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present ♥ Military organisations protected outlying regions. [167]

Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[168] The switch-over did not occur until the end of the Kediri Kingdom: it was the Singhasari Kingdom that witnessed 'the decline of Hindu culture and civilisation in Java and the succession of Javanese culture.'[169] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'banner, ... soldier, officer'.[170]

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Lottie Field; Enrico Cioni ♥ LF coded an earlier, pre-expert version of this section.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ temple founding ceremony ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children. All had gathered to take part in a ceremony sponsored by a local noble, who was present with his wife and three sons. The occasion was the founding of a new temple.//Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java. [...]//These ceremonies normally followed a standard format, and that of December 27, 902 C.E., did not deviate from it. The ritual did not take long to complete. First, the noble sponsoring the building of the temple, who is referred to in the account by his tide, Rakai Panggumulan, distributed presents to all the people who had come. The distribution of gifts was an important symbolic act; ancient Indonesians nobility spent much of their energy endeavoring to acquire precious items that they could then redistribute to loyal followers as tokens of spiritual merit. The scribe recorded the noble's bounty in detail. Next, all participants decorated themselves with flowers and paint; we can only try to imagine the colors and patterns they would have drawn upon their bodies. A sacred stone was set in the center of the space where the temple would be built. Offerings were then laid upon it. The people next sat in a circle around the stone; a priest intoned curses against anyone who would dare disturb the peace of their village. With that, the brief ceremony was concluded.//Thereupon began a celebration that probably took longer than the ritual itself. The nobles, elders, sculptor, and others, many of whom were probably commoners from the village, indulged in feasting enlivened by supplies of palm wine, probably drunk from bamboo cups. The repast was succeeded by music and dancing, possibly of a solemn nature; but the Javanese sense of humor was perhaps already as well developed as it is today, for as usual there was also a performance by a buffoon. No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [191]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [192]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [193]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [194]
♠ Duration ♣ [7-8] ♥ hours. This is a guess provided by an expert, John Miksic. [195]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ unknown ♥ Numbers for this could not be provided by expert (John Miksic) [196]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children." [197]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ temple founding ceremony ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children. All had gathered to take part in a ceremony sponsored by a local noble, who was present with his wife and three sons. The occasion was the founding of a new temple.//Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java. [...]//These ceremonies normally followed a standard format, and that of December 27, 902 C.E., did not deviate from it. The ritual did not take long to complete. First, the noble sponsoring the building of the temple, who is referred to in the account by his tide, Rakai Panggumulan, distributed presents to all the people who had come. The distribution of gifts was an important symbolic act; ancient Indonesians nobility spent much of their energy endeavoring to acquire precious items that they could then redistribute to loyal followers as tokens of spiritual merit. The scribe recorded the noble's bounty in detail. Next, all participants decorated themselves with flowers and paint; we can only try to imagine the colors and patterns they would have drawn upon their bodies. A sacred stone was set in the center of the space where the temple would be built. Offerings were then laid upon it. The people next sat in a circle around the stone; a priest intoned curses against anyone who would dare disturb the peace of their village. With that, the brief ceremony was concluded.//Thereupon began a celebration that probably took longer than the ritual itself. The nobles, elders, sculptor, and others, many of whom were probably commoners from the village, indulged in feasting enlivened by supplies of palm wine, probably drunk from bamboo cups. The repast was succeeded by music and dancing, possibly of a solemn nature; but the Javanese sense of humor was perhaps already as well developed as it is today, for as usual there was also a performance by a buffoon. No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [229]

♠ pain ♣ inferred absent ♥ The ritual seems to have been largely euphoric: "Thereupon began a celebration that probably took longer than the ritual itself. The nobles, elders, sculptor, and others, many of whom were probably commoners from the village, indulged in feasting enlivened by supplies of palm wine, probably drunk from bamboo cups. The repast was succeeded by music and dancing, possibly of a solemn nature; but the Javanese sense of humor was perhaps already as well developed as it is today, for as usual there was also a performance by a buffoon. No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [243]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ puja ♥ From the sixth century CE onwards, the puja becomes the main form of Hindu worship, and it involves making offerings (water, unguents, sandalwood paste, incense, food, honey, valuable objects, dairy products) to an image of the deity, and bathing it, as well as chanting, and sharing small quantities of food with other participants [268].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ Four times daily. Temple puja takes place at sunrise, noon, sunset, and midnight [269].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ Four times daily. Temple puja takes place at sunrise, noon, sunset, and midnight [270].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ Four times daily. Temple puja takes place at sunrise, noon, sunset, and midnight [271]. Thomas Cressy: Coded as not applicable due to what has been coded in Cambodian versions of this ritual, it seems there were no passive spectators.
♠ Duration ♣ unknown ♥ hour. It is likely this detail cannot be known due to a lack of documentation [272].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ unknown ♥ It is likely this detail cannot be known due to a lack of documentation [273].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ Priests at Hindu temples normally belong to the Brahmin caste [274].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ unknown ♥ It is likely this detail cannot be known due to a lack of documentation [275].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed. Thomas Cressy: Coded as not applicable due to what has been coded in Cambodian versions of this ritual, it seems there were no passive spectators.

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ temple founding ceremony ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children. All had gathered to take part in a ceremony sponsored by a local noble, who was present with his wife and three sons. The occasion was the founding of a new temple.//Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java. [...]//These ceremonies normally followed a standard format, and that of December 27, 902 C.E., did not deviate from it. The ritual did not take long to complete. First, the noble sponsoring the building of the temple, who is referred to in the account by his tide, Rakai Panggumulan, distributed presents to all the people who had come. The distribution of gifts was an important symbolic act; ancient Indonesians nobility spent much of their energy endeavoring to acquire precious items that they could then redistribute to loyal followers as tokens of spiritual merit. The scribe recorded the noble's bounty in detail. Next, all participants decorated themselves with flowers and paint; we can only try to imagine the colors and patterns they would have drawn upon their bodies. A sacred stone was set in the center of the space where the temple would be built. Offerings were then laid upon it. The people next sat in a circle around the stone; a priest intoned curses against anyone who would dare disturb the peace of their village. With that, the brief ceremony was concluded.//Thereupon began a celebration that probably took longer than the ritual itself. The nobles, elders, sculptor, and others, many of whom were probably commoners from the village, indulged in feasting enlivened by supplies of palm wine, probably drunk from bamboo cups. The repast was succeeded by music and dancing, possibly of a solemn nature; but the Javanese sense of humor was perhaps already as well developed as it is today, for as usual there was also a performance by a buffoon. No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [307]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [308]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [309]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [310]
♠ Duration ♣ [7-8] ♥ hours. This is a guess provided by an expert, John Miksic. [311]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ unknown ♥ Numbers for this could not be provided by expert (John Miksic) [312]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children." [313]

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Institutional Variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Lottie Field; Jenny Reddish ♥

JR: We have very little information about the Kadiri royal succession, or even its political history more generally. 'At present next to nothing is known about the kingdom of Janggala, but the level of knowledge of the kingdom of Pangjalu or Kadiri is not much better. As Coedès (1968: 167) notes, "the history of the states of Indonesia during this whole period is singularly colorless". What has come down to us is a handful of names of Kadiri kings, most of which are unread and unpublished, except for their calendrical information...'.[346]

♠ Editor ♣ ♥

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ 'The king's sovereignty was also maintained by measures, taken by the central government, to strengthen the state's military power'.[347]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ Extensive reading around this topic revealed no reference to any kind of independent legislature in the sources. Pigeaud asserts that 'In all periods of Javanese history rulers issued orders and rules pertaining to special aspects of social life, in the first place the social organization of their own Courts and residences, the Royal revenue, and agrarian administration'.[348] As well as charters conferring certain rights and obligations (for instance, tax-free sima status), Old Javanese epigraphy includes 'royal edicts and proclamations', which 'generally begin with the word wruhé or ājñā, Sanskrit for "order, command".'[349] Specifically for the Kediri kingdom, Sedyawati says that 'Transgression of the king's, that is, the state's, regulations was not only threatened by curses, as was normally the case in earlier periods, but in the Kadiri period it was also sanctioned by imposing fines, consisting of a certain amount of gold'.[350] Here, Sedyawati explicitly equates the law of the king with the law of the state.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent ♥ Hall says in a general discussion of pre-1500 Southeast Asian kingship that 'While in a theoretical sense the king's only duty was to maintain the world order - to protect his realm, promote its prosperity, and facilitate passage to the realm of the ancestors - in practice the monarch's duties sometimes involved the application of customary law regarding land and labor.'[351]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ present ♥ In twelfth-century inscriptions, locals are said to 'petition the dust of the king's shoes'.[352] Moreover, in the Kediri Kingdom, a new state official called the sopana appears, whose job was to act 'as an intermediary between the king and those who needed the king's favor'.[353]
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ [absent; present] ♥ Writing about the late Medang period, Kinney refers a 'Javanese-style council of military commanders and Hindu and Buddhist priests' being instituted in Bali, implying that this council was already part of the Javanese government by 1000 CE.[354] Perhaps such a body could criticize and advise the king, but extensive reading around this topic revealed no evidence that this was a formal right.
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ Extensive reading around this topic revealed no reference in the sources to a formal right of any non-governmental, non-religious group to criticize the king.

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ [absent; present] ♥ Writing about the late Medang period, Kinney refers a 'Javanese-style council of military commanders and Hindu and Buddhist priests' being instituted in Bali, implying that this council was already part of the Javanese government by 1000 CE.[355] However, Extensive reading around this topic revealed no account of the rights and duties of this body, so it is unclear whether members had the formal right to to impose sanctions.
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ Extensive reading around this topic revealed no evidence that non-governmental, non-religious bodies had a formal right to impose sanctions on the Kediri kings.
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Writing about the late Medang period, Kinney refers a 'Javanese-style council of military commanders and Hindu and Buddhist priests' being instituted in Bali, implying that this council was already part of the Javanese government by 1000 CE.[356] However, extensive reading around this topic revealed no account of the rights and duties of this body, so it is unclear whether members had the formal right to constrain the monarch's actions.
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ Extensive reading around this topic revealed no evidence that non-governmental, non-religious bodies had a formal right to constraint the Kediri kings' actions.
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ Extensive reading around this topic revealed no mention in the sources of any legal mechanism for removing the monarch from power.

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ Sedyawati says that a high-ranking religious official called the 'panajyan sri maharaja (teacher of the king)', was 'closest to the king' in the Kediri period. 'He seems to have had a special status, supposedly that of the king's adviser in religious affairs'.[357] Although this man was a religious agent, a 'master of yoga in the bhairawa path', as a 'high ranking official' I think we can also count him as part of the government - indeed, the 'true bureaucratic leaders' in the Kediri kingdom included officials specializing in religious affairs.[358] Perhaps such a close and respected advisor would have been able to express disapproval of the monarch's actions.
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ Sedyawati says that a high-ranking religious official called the 'panajyan sri maharaja (teacher of the king)', was 'closest to the king' in the Kediri period. 'He seems to have had a special status, supposedly that of the king's adviser in religious affairs'.[359] Although this man was a religious agent, a 'master of yoga in the bhairawa path', as a 'high ranking official' I think we can also count him as part of the government - indeed, the 'true bureaucratic leaders' in the Kediri kingdom included officials specializing in religious affairs.[360] Perhaps such a close and respected advisor would have been able to withhold co-operation or impose other informal sanctions on the kings' decisions.
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ Javanese monarchs in the 9th to 13th centuries CE 'did not normally have local land rights, and even their tax rights were subject to social restraint'.[361] Wisseman Christie states that 'The ruler had no power to remove tax rights from his fellow rakais' (lords with tax rights over groups of villages).[362]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not found any references in the sources to informal impeachment, but it is worth noting that we know very little about the Kediri kings' reigns. 'For many years after Airlangga's death [in 1049] Javanese history is almost a blank ... Ten kings are mentioned up to 1222, but most are mere names'.[363]

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ Sedyawati says that a high-ranking religious official called the 'panajyan sri maharaja (teacher of the king)', was 'closest to the king' in the Kediri period. 'He seems to have had a special status, supposedly that of the king's adviser in religious affairs'.[364] Perhaps such a close and respected advisor would have been able to express disapproval of the monarch's actions.
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ Sedyawati says that a high-ranking religious official called the 'panajyan sri maharaja (teacher of the king)', was 'closest to the king' in the Kediri period. 'He seems to have had a special status, supposedly that of the king's adviser in religious affairs'.[365] This man was a 'master of yoga in the bhairawa path' as well as a 'high ranking official'. Perhaps such a close and respected advisor would have been able to withhold co-operation or impose other sanctions on the kings' decisions, but I have not found any evidence that this was a formal right.
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ Sedyawati says that a high-ranking religious official called the 'panajyan sri maharaja (teacher of the king)', was 'closest to the king' in the Kediri period. 'He seems to have had a special status, supposedly that of the king's adviser in religious affairs'.[366] This man was a 'master of yoga in the bhairawa path' as well as a 'high ranking official'. Perhaps such a close and respected advisor would have been able to impose informal constraints on the kings' actions, but I have not found any evidence that this was a formal right. More generally, Reid discusses the relationship of kings in the 'charter era' of 900-1300 CE to Buddhist and Hindu religious specialists and concepts: 'Popular Buddhism remained a key mobilizing factor, but kings were increasingly able to channel it for their own accumulation of sacred power. And for such kings, even while patronizing the Buddhist pilgrimage centers, the hierarchies of Hinduism were indispensable. They required a corps of Brahmins to conduct the rituals of enthronement and legitimation as godlike devaraja or "king of the gods," elevated far above others by the accumulation of supernatural power.'[367] There is a strong sense here that the kings relied on the support of holy men and sacred institutions.
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ inferred present ♥ Reid discusses the relationship of kings in the 'charter era' of 900-1300 CE to Buddhist and Hindu religious specialists and concepts: 'Popular Buddhism remained a key mobilizing factor, but kings were increasingly able to channel it for their own accumulation of sacred power. And for such kings, even while patronizing the Buddhist pilgrimage centers, the hierarchies of Hinduism were indispensable. They required a corps of Brahmins to conduct the rituals of enthronement and legitimation as godlike devaraja or "king of the gods," elevated far above others by the accumulation of supernatural power.'[368] There is a strong sense here that the kings relied on the support of holy men and sacred institutions.
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not found any references in the sources to informal or formal impeachment, but it is worth noting that we know very little about the Kediri kings' reigns. 'For many years after Airlangga's death [in 1049] Javanese history is almost a blank ... Ten kings are mentioned up to 1222, but most are mere names'.[369]
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ present ♥ Hall says that 'the Kadiri kings stressed their aggressive pursuit of the betterment of humankind, both by their actions in the world and also, if necessary, through their appeals to the Divine on humankind's behalf.'[370] From this we can infer that the Kadiri kings recognized a higher power above them.
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ Kulke describes religiously motivated state spending on public ceremonies in the early Southeast Asian kingdoms: 'The importance of the personal belief of the rulers and their priests and their magical capability is known to us already from the earliest inscriptional and literary evidence. And on special occasions there were always grand ceremonies, for example, seasonal festivities, potlatch-like "Verdienstfeste" or Vedic rituals'.[371]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ absent ♥ Wolters says of early Java in general that 'Rules of royal succession are unclear, which means that it would be inappropriate to infer "dynastic" succession rules'.[372] Heidhues comments that 'Much of the instability of early Southeast Asian realms resulted from internal factors. Important was the lack of fixed rules for royal succession.'[373]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ absent ♥ Wolters says of early Java in general that 'Rules of royal succession are unclear, which means that it would be inappropriate to infer "dynastic" succession rules'.[374] Heidhues comments that 'Much of the instability of early Southeast Asian realms resulted from internal factors. Important was the lack of fixed rules for royal succession.'[375]
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ [6-9] ♥ 'For many years after Airlangga's death [in 1049] Javanese history is almost a blank ... Ten kings are mentioned up to 1222, but most are mere names'.[376] Coedès provides a shorter king list with some rough dates: 0. Bameswara (1117-30 CE);[377] 1. Warmeswara-Jayabhaya (r. 1135-57);[378] 2. Sarwweswara ('who was reigning in 1159-61');[379] 3. Arryeswara ('who was reigning in 1171');[380] 4. Kroncaryyadipa (also known as Gandra, he 'was reigning in 1181');[381] 5. Kameswara (known from inscriptions of 1182 and 1185);[382] 6. Srengga-Jayawarsa/Warsajaya, also known as Kertajaya (known from inscriptions between 1194 and 1205).[383][384] According to the Pararaton (the Javanese 'Book of Kings', written in the late 15th century),[385] he was the last king of Kediri, dethroned by Ken Angrok.[386]
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ suspected unknown ♥ We do not have enough evidence to decide which of the Kadiri transitions were contested: 'For many years after Airlangga's death [in 1049] Javanese history is almost a blank ... Ten kings are mentioned up to 1222, but most are mere names'.[387] Hall comments that 'Following Airlangga's death, the Java monarchy split into competing factions, with kings identified by their association with two rival courts. One regional dynasty ruled from Kadiri on the southwest edge of the east Java river plain, and the other at Singhasari to the southeast on the Malang Plateau'.[388]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ suspected unknown ♥ We do not have enough evidence to decide which of the Kadiri transitions represented successful overturns: 'For many years after Airlangga's death [in 1049] Javanese history is almost a blank ... Ten kings are mentioned up to 1222, but most are mere names'.[389] Hall comments that 'Following Airlangga's death, the Java monarchy split into competing factions, with kings identified by their association with two rival courts. One regional dynasty ruled from Kadiri on the southwest edge of the east Java river plain, and the other at Singhasari to the southeast on the Malang Plateau'.[390]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

JR: I'm hoping that the experts might be able to help us code the empty variables and improve on others here. From the English-language sources I have been able to access, the structure of the state- and local-level administration (and the dividing line between these two groups) is not completely clear to me. It would be useful to know whether Schrieke's 'patrimonial bureaucracy' model for precolonial Java is still considered applicable to this period by contemporary historians and archaeologists. Access to large volumes of inscriptions such as Sarkar's Corpus of the Inscriptions of Java may also help to clarify these issues.[391]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥[392] The Kediri Kingdom saw 'noteworthy developments ... in state management'.[393] For instance, we have evidence of a 'special government official', the senapati sarwwajala, with responsibilities related to the system of water management.[394]

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ equal ♥ In Schrieke's model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom' (which, Anderson comments, 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'), 'Within the central government latent or overt tension persists between the royal descent group, or the ruler's extended family, and the ministeriales, high-ranking officials of common origin who have risen to power on the basis of their administrative capacities and personal loyalty to the ruler'.[395] It seems to me that in this model - excluding the royal family - there is no real division between state-level officials and 'top nobility'.

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ inferred present ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[396] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim':[397] Hall says that 'By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command "emanated from the maharāja's shoes" through expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority',[398] though his power may have been mediated through these 'networks', clearly the king could issue direct orders to his administrators.
♠ Indirect ♣ inferred absent ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[399] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim':[400] Hall says that 'By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command "emanated from the maharāja's shoes" through expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority',[401] though his power may have been mediated through these 'networks', clearly the king could issue direct orders to his administrators.
♠ Diffused ♣ inferred absent ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[402] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim':[403] Hall says that 'By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command "emanated from the maharāja's shoes" through expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority',[404] though his power may have been mediated through these 'networks', clearly the king could issue direct orders to his administrators.
♠ Mixed ♣ inferred absent ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[405] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. ♠ Governmental control ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', 'the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim'.[408] There doesn't seem to be a clear distinction between state-level administrators and members of government.
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not found any indication in the sources that state officials were subject to oversight from any non-governmental body.

Source of Support of state-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Governed population ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Land ♣ present ♥ Hermann Kulke has positioned himself in opposition to the 'conventional school' of Southeast Asian historians: he thinks they have too readily applied 'more or less all Weberian criteria of a modern State' to early Southeast Asian states.[409] From this standpoint, he argues that 'the court and its officers in all these [early Southeast Asian] States lived mainly from their own landed property or from benefices with an usufruct which might have been limited in time or heredity ... the court of the Early Kingdom depended mainly on the resources of its direct hinterland'.[410] However, he does not provide more detailed analysis of this for the early Javanese kingdoms. In Schrieke's model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', discussed by Anderson, 'Payment of officials is essentially in the form of benefices allotted by the ruler for the period of tenure of each particular office'.[411] To follow the Encyclopædia Britannica's definition of a 'benefice', this would have entailed a royal lease of land to support the administrator for the period of office.[412]

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

JR: I'm hoping that the experts might be able to help us code the empty variables and improve on others here. From the English-language sources I have been able to access, the structure of the state- and local-level administration (and the dividing line between these two groups) is not completely clear to me. It would be useful to know whether Schrieke's 'patrimonial bureaucracy' model for precolonial Java is still considered applicable to this period by contemporary historians and archaeologists. Access to large volumes of inscriptions such as Sarkar's Corpus of the Inscriptions of Java may also help to clarify these issues.[415]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ suspected unknown ♥ 'In the Kadiri territorial administration, the bhumi [kingdom] was further subdivided into regions containing several thani [villages]. This regional unity above the thani level was called wisaya, whereas the central thani functioning as the capital of the wisaya was called dalem thani. There was a varied amount of thani within one wisaya'.[416] Sedyawati comments that this clustering of villages into higher-level territorial units was a 'Kadiri invention'.[417]

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥ In the Kediri period, people known as duwan or duhan, who served as middlemen between the central government and the village communities, 'were officials, part of the governing élite on village level'.[418]

Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ absent ♥ 'During this era [i.e. after Airlangga] kings came to possess a degree of direct administrative control over their subordinate regions in east Java that went beyond that of previous courts'.[419] However, Wisseman Christie's discussion of administrative developments in Javanese polities in general before the 15th century CE makes clear that local officials would have retained substantial autonomy: 'Javanese rulers of successive states seem to have resorted, like their later counterparts, to tax farming as a means of acquiring income without reforming the very decentralized tax-collecting system'.[420] Similarly, M. Boechari concluded that 'what remained unchanged during the whole [old-Javanese] period was that there never had been a centralized government. The kingdom was divided into a number of autonomous areas, governed by rakais or rakrayans [who] could act independently from the king'.[421] In light of what the sources say, I think we could posit some indirect (rather than completely diffused) royal control of local administration in the Kediri period, but coding 'direct', implying tight control over hiring and firing and the duties of local officials, would perhaps not be justified.
♠ Indirect ♣ present ♥ 'During this era [i.e. after Airlangga] kings came to possess a degree of direct administrative control over their subordinate regions in east Java that went beyond that of previous courts'.[422] However, Wisseman Christie's discussion of administrative developments in Javanese polities in general before the 15th century CE makes clear that local officials would have retained substantial autonomy: 'Javanese rulers of successive states seem to have resorted, like their later counterparts, to tax farming as a means of acquiring income without reforming the very decentralized tax-collecting system'.[423] Similarly, M. ♠ Mixed ♣ present ♥ 'During this era [i.e. after Airlangga] kings came to possess a degree of direct administrative control over their subordinate regions in east Java that went beyond that of previous courts'.[427] However, Wisseman Christie's discussion of administrative developments in Javanese polities in general before the 15th century CE makes clear that local officials would have retained substantial autonomy: 'Javanese rulers of successive states seem to have resorted, like their later counterparts, to tax farming as a means of acquiring income without reforming the very decentralized tax-collecting system'.[428] Similarly, M. Boechari concluded that 'what remained unchanged during the whole [old-Javanese] period was that there never had been a centralized government. The kingdom was divided into a number of autonomous areas, governed by rakais or rakrayans [who] could act independently from the king'.[429] State power over local administration in this period - though perhaps more direct than before - was thus still diffused to some extent.
