Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ present ♥ 'Within the state of Kadiri there was a dualism of authority between the rajya level authority and the thani-wisaya [village-group of villages] level respectively. Both were, however, integrated within the administrative structure of the state. This duality was moreover represented by the distinction of the source of the law for each level of authority. There was the law of the villages beside the law of the state'.[432] There were forms of customary law - sukha-dukha - 'of the rajya' (the state) and 'of the thani' (the local village): Sedyawati thinks 'It can be assumed that the sukha-dukha of the thani was the indigenous customary law, while the sukha-dukha of the rajya was the customary law adapted to or imbued with elements from the Hindu dandaniti (administration of justice ...)'.[433]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ inferred present ♥ Zakharov comments that the inscriptions of Jayapattra Diedukman (907 CE) and Jayapattra of Vurudu Kudul (922 CE), from the preceding Medang period, show 'a legal system supported by the state'.[434] From the evidence of the jayapattra, Hoadley has argued for continuity in Javanese legal tradition from the early 10th right up until to the 18th century,[435] so I think we can infer that the state under the Kediri kings would have continued to support law courts.

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ present ♥ Jayapattra, '"certificates of victory"' awarded to the winners of lawsuits', were issued in Java from the at least the early 10th to the 18th centuries CE.[436] A jayapattra typically included 'a detailed description of the judicial process by which it came into being'.[437]

Accessibility of the Legal System

♠ ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ A representative of the ruling elite, a royal official (nayaka) appeared in the tax dispute case recorded in the Palepangan inscription of 906 CE, in the preceding Medang period. The elders (ramanta) of Palepangan complained that this official had 'taxed more lands than the community had'.[438] From the evidence of the jayapattra, Hoadley has argued for continuity in Javanese legal tradition from the early 10th right up until to the 18th century,[439] so I think we can infer that the ruling elite would have maintained its access to the legal system under the Kediri kings.
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ The Palepangan inscription of 906 CE (from the earlier Medang period) records a dispute over taxation levels between village elders (ramanta) and a royal official. The elders of Palepangan complained that the 'royal official (nayaka)' had 'taxed more lands than the community had',[440] showing that these local elites had access to the legal system and could bring cases on behalf of their communities. 'The elders (rāmanta) asked the rakryan mapatiḥ of Hino [one of the high officials in the state of Matarām, a "prime minister" (Barrett Jones 1984: 99)] to take the case.'[441] Zakharov notes that in these records of taxation disputes, it is only the elders (no '[o]ther commoners') who appear. 'The commoners of Palepangan, perhaps, were interested in tax reductions but it remains uncertain whether the elders acted in cooperation with them or not.'[442] Hoadley has argued for continuity in Javanese legal tradition from the early 10th right up until to the 18th century,[443] so I think we can infer that the local elite would have maintained its access to the legal system under the Kediri kings.
♠ general population ♣ inferred present ♥ Zakharov comments that 'We know of at least two cases of legal assistance for an individual in ancient Java.' For instance, 'the inscription of Jayapattra Diedukman dated from 907 [from the earlier Medang period] records the case of the debt of a woman who passed away ... The creditor wanted to recover the debt from her husband but the latter did not know about his wife's debt and, according to Old Javanese law, he should not have been a defendant in this case because the debt concerned his wife's property'.[444] Hoadley has argued for continuity in Javanese legal tradition from the early 10th right up until to the 18th century,[445] so I think we can infer that the general population would have maintained their access to the legal system under the Kediri kings.
♠ foreign groups ♣ ♥ JR: It has been difficult to find evidence of how foreigners were treated under Old Javanese law. I have included a question about this for the experts in the document for the Singapore workshop. The sima charter of Cane, from 1021 CE (slightly before this period), declared that a variety of kilalan ('non-resident') individuals such as 'Kalingas, Āryyas, Singhalese, Pandikiras, Dravidians, Chams, Khmers...' were subject to the authority of 'the holy water foundation of Padada ri Dawuhan' for the duration of their stay in the Cane community. This foundation could impose fines on the visitors.[446] However, also classed as kilalan are members of the royal household,[447] showing that the category was not designed to curtail the legal rights of foreign groups in particular.
♠ ethnic groups ♣ ♥ It has been difficult to find evidence of how different ethnic groups were treated under Old Javanese law, but the outcome of the case recorded in the Wurudu Kidul jayapattra of 907 CE (in the earlier Medang period) suggests that being a member of an established wanua (irrigated rice-farming community) conferred special legal rights - 'Only a member of the community (anak wanua) enjoyed full rights in the kěraton society.'[448][449] Writing on precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, Scott notes that '[e]cological niche, because it marks off different subsistence routines, rituals, and material culture, is one distinction around which ethnogenesis can occur'.[450] In an ancient Javanese context, this could have meant separate ethnic identities for rice-cultivating anak wanua and swidden cultivators living at higher altitudes, but I have not found concrete evidence for this.

Accessibility of Trials

♠ ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ A representative of the ruling elite, a royal official (nayaka) appeared in the tax dispute case recorded in the Palepangan inscription of 906 CE, in the preceding Medang period. The elders (ramanta) of Palepangan complained that this official had 'taxed more lands than the community had'.[451] From the evidence of the jayapattra, Hoadley has argued for continuity in Javanese legal tradition from the early 10th right up until to the 18th century,[452] so I think we can infer that the ruling elite would have maintained its access to the legal system under the Kediri kings.
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ The Palepangan inscription of 906 CE (from the earlier Medang period) records a dispute over taxation levels between village elders (ramanta) and a royal official. The elders of Palepangan complained that the 'royal official (nayaka)' had 'taxed more lands than the community had',[453] showing that these local elites had access to the legal system and could bring cases on behalf of their communities. 'The elders (rāmanta) asked the rakryan mapatiḥ of Hino [one of the high officials in the state of Matarām, a "prime minister" (Barrett Jones 1984: 99)] to take the case.'[454] Zakharov notes that in these records of taxation disputes, it is only the elders (no '[o]ther commoners') who appear. 'The commoners of Palepangan, perhaps, were interested in tax reductions but it remains uncertain whether the elders acted in cooperation with them or not.'[455] Hoadley has argued for continuity in Javanese legal tradition from the early 10th right up until to the 18th century,[456] so I think we can infer that the local elite would have maintained its access to the legal system under the Kediri kings.
♠ general population ♣ inferred present ♥ Zakharov comments that 'We know of at least two cases of legal assistance for an individual in ancient Java.' For instance, 'the inscription of Jayapattra Diedukman dated from 907 [from the earlier Medang period] records the case of the debt of a woman who passed away ... The creditor wanted to recover the debt from her husband but the latter did not know about his wife's debt and, according to Old Javanese law, he should not have been a defendant in this case because the debt concerned his wife's property'.[457] Hoadley has argued for continuity in Javanese legal tradition from the early 10th right up until to the 18th century,[458] so I think we can infer that the general population would have maintained their access to the legal system under the Kediri kings.
♠ foreign groups ♣ ♥ JR: It has been difficult to find evidence of how foreigners were treated under Old Javanese law. I have included a question about this for the experts in the document for the Singapore workshop. The sima charter of Cane, from 1021 CE (slightly before this period), declared that a variety of kilalan ('non-resident') individuals such as 'Kalingas, Āryyas, Singhalese, Pandikiras, Dravidians, Chams, Khmers...' were subject to the authority of 'the holy water foundation of Padada ri Dawuhan' for the duration of their stay in the Cane community. This foundation could impose fines on the visitors.[459] However, also classed as kilalan are members of the royal household,[460] showing that the category was not designed to curtail the legal rights of foreign groups in particular.
♠ ethnic groups ♣ ♥ It has been difficult to find evidence of how different ethnic groups were treated under Old Javanese law, but the outcome of the case recorded in the Wurudu Kidul jayapattra of 907 CE (in the earlier Medang period) suggests that being a member of an established wanua (irrigated rice-farming community) conferred special legal rights - 'Only a member of the community (anak wanua) enjoyed full rights in the kěraton society.'[461][462] Writing on precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, Scott notes that '[e]cological niche, because it marks off different subsistence routines, rituals, and material culture, is one distinction around which ethnogenesis can occur'.[463] In an ancient Javanese context, this could have meant separate ethnic identities for rice-cultivating anak wanua and swidden cultivators living at higher altitudes, but I have not found concrete evidence for this.

Accessibility of Courts

♠ Courts in polity capital ♣ inferred present ♥ Wisseman-Christie has argued that in pre-Majapahit Java, villages handled 'most of their own judicial affairs. A few legal matters appear to have reached higher courts, but those recorded arose from inter-village disputes or differences over taxes owed to the state'.[464] I think we can infer that these higher courts were located at Airlangga's royal centre - there were no 'regional capitals' during this period because there was no settlement hierarchy,[465] and we know that the earlier Medang kings heard cases in their courts.[466]
♠ Courts in regional capitals ♣ absent ♥ There were no regional capitals during this period because there was no settlement hierarchy.[467] 'The only concentrated accumulations of population to appear before the fourteenth century seem to have developed around one or two ports, and even these concentrations seem to have fallen short of the size and stability that characterize true urban centers'.[468]
♠ Courts in provincial towns ♣ present ♥ Wisseman-Christie has argued that in pre-Majapahit Java, villages handled 'most of their own judicial affairs. A few legal matters appear to have reached higher courts, but those recorded arose from inter-village disputes or differences over taxes owed to the state'.[469]

♠ Typical speed of judicial proceedings in criminal cases ♣ unknown ♥ Hoadley's discussion of the jayapattra legal documents in pre-Islamic Java covers only two 10th-century inscriptions and 'the two Madjapahit examples of the genre', with no mention of any jayapattra from the Kahuripan or Kediri periods.[470] The Medang examples provide evidence for judicial process in legal cases (there is no clear division between criminal and civil cases at this time), but their style is much terser than later (Majapahit-era) examples. They show 'compression of time ... mere listing of events ... omission of unimportant details'.[471] As far as I can tell, they do not tell us how long the proceedings took.

♠ Typical speed of judicial proceedings in civil cases ♣ unknown ♥ Hoadley's discussion of the jayapattra legal documents in pre-Islamic Java covers only two 10th-century inscriptions and 'the two Madjapahit examples of the genre', with no mention of any jayapattra from the Kahuripan or Kediri periods.[472] The Medang examples provide evidence for judicial process in legal cases (there is no clear division between criminal and civil cases at this time), but their style is much terser than later (Majapahit-era) examples. They show 'compression of time ... mere listing of events ... omission of unimportant details'.[473] As far as I can tell, they do not tell us how long the proceedings took.

Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ present ♥ Wisseman-Christie has argued that in pre-Majapahit Java, villages handled 'most of their own judicial affairs. A few legal matters appear to have reached higher courts, but those recorded arose from inter-village disputes or differences over taxes owed to the state'.[474]

♠ Religious judiciary ♣ [absent; present] ♥ I have not come across any explicit mention of a religious judiciary in the Kediri period. However, the sima charter of Cane, from 1021 CE (slightly before the Kediri period), declared that a variety of kilalan ('non-resident') individuals such as 'Kalingas, Āryyas, Singhalese, Pandikiras, Dravidians, Chams, Khmers...' were subject to the authority of 'the holy water foundation of Padada ri Dawuhan', a religious organization, for the duration of their stay in the Cane community. This foundation could impose fines on the visitors.[475]

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ present ♥ 'Land in early Java was owned by village communities, by families, and by individuals.'[476] In an earlier article, Christie has also said that the Old Javanese wanua communities were 'administered by a council of heads of land-owning households - the karaman - a number of whom held named community offices'.[477]

♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ Speaking generally about the pre-1500 states of Southeast Asia, Hall describes landholding patterns in the area around the capital: 'The king was usually a major landholder in this core, but the landholding rights of others - normally familial or individual rights to a share of the produce from the land under their authority rather than ownership in the modern sense - were also protected.[478]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ ♥
♠ Partibility ♣ inferred present ♥ Wisseman Christie says that 'complicated marriage settlements, in a society whose patterns of inheritance were bilateral and did not necessarily favour either the first born or male descendants, almost certainly played a major role in the fragmentation process' (of the watĕk groups of villages).[479] This seems to imply that landholdings could be split up.
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ Wisseman Christie says that 'By the tenth century [in Java], sawah fields were those most often cited as having been pawned or leased'.[480] Her implication seems to be that the practice of leasing then continued into later centuries.
♠ Lease Rights ♣ ♥
♠ Transfer of property ♣ present ♥ Wisseman Christie thinks it is 'clear' that by the early 9th century in Java, irrigated wet-rice fields were 'individually owned, heritable, and alienable.'[481]
♠ Use of property ♣ ♥
♠ Property market ♣ present ♥ Wisseman Christie thinks it is 'clear' that by the early 9th century in Java, irrigated wet-rice fields were 'individually owned, heritable, and alienable.'[482]

♠ Other property ownership ♣ ♥

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ inferred present ♥ Wisseman Christie says that 'complicated marriage settlements, in a society whose patterns of inheritance were bilateral and did not necessarily favour either the first born or male descendants, almost certainly played a major role in the fragmentation process' (of the watĕk groups of villages).[483] This seems to imply that landholdings could be split up.
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ absent ♥ Patterns of inheritance were bilateral in the early Javanese kingdoms.[484]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ absent ♥ Patterns of inheritance were bilateral in the early Javanese kingdoms.[485]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ absent ♥ Patterns of inheritance in the early Javanese kingdoms 'did not necessarily favour either the first born or male descendants'.[486]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ absent ♥ Patterns of inheritance in the early Javanese kingdoms 'did not necessarily favour either the first born or male descendants'.[487]
♠ other inheritance practices ♣ present ♥ Patterns of inheritance were bilateral in the early Javanese kingdoms.[488] Hall comments that 'Bilateral kinship was a common feature of the early Southeast Asian socioeconomy. In determining inheritance, for instance, equal value was accorded the mother's and father's relatives, and sons and daughters usually both received rights to their deceased parents' estates (including land). The value of daughters was never questioned in early Southeast Asia.'[489]
♠ Trusts ♣ ♥
♠ State expropriation ♣ absent ♥ Rights to communal wanua land were passed down within village communities, derived from 'deified ancestors', and the state seems to have respected this: 'The wanua in this sense was acknowledged by the court to have had clearly identified boundaries and to have included all of the land within those borders, cultivated or not'.[490] Hall's (1992) discussion shows that Javanese monarchs in the 9th to 13th centuries CE were subject to certain customary restrictions on their rights to land and tax revenue.[491] 'When title to land was to be transferred, that land was purchased first from those villages affected'.[492]
♠ Non-state expropriation ♣ inferred absent ♥ Wisseman Christie's discussion of sima transfers implies that religious foundations as well as Javanese rulers and local lords (rakai) respected villagers' rights to land, and would not take possession of it without compensating them first: 'If actual title to land was to be transferred to a religious foundation, then that land was first purchased, for gold, from the villagers affected'.[493]

Equity[edit]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ present ♥ Studies suggest that large bridewealth payments were found in areas with patrilineal kin groups and are mentioned as existing in Java prior to 1830 in nineteenth-century literature. It has been suggested that high brideprices had been conducive to late female marriages and significant proportions of women never marrying at all which has been in turn been linked to low population growth. It seems unlikely under these circumstances that many would have been able to afford multiple wives.[494] Reid refers to a 'widespread Southeast Asian pattern of monogamy within marriage but relatively easy divorce and remarriage' in the period before the arrival of Islam.[495] West says in a general discussion of Javanese culture that 'the nobility and prijaji [bureaucrats] have traditionally practiced polygyny, the ability to have more than one wife, while most other Javanese have not done so'.[496]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ The division of Airlangga's realm resolved a dispute between sons born of two royal consorts.[497] Creese has examined the portrayal of women in the kakawin literary epics of the 'Indic courts of Java and Bali' (9th-19th centuries CE) and comments that '[i]n the kakawin world, polygamy is the norm'.[498] We can perhaps infer from this that the Javanese court elite would have considered polygyny (but not polyandry)[499] an acceptable form of marriage. West also says in a general discussion of Javanese culture that 'the nobility and prijaji [bureaucrats] have traditionally practiced polygyny, the ability to have more than one wife, while most other Javanese have not done so'.[500]
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ Creese has examined the portrayal of women in the kakawin literary epics of the 'Indic courts of Java and Bali' (9th-19th centuries CE) and comments that '[i]n the kakawin world, polygamy is the norm'.[501] We can perhaps infer from this that the Javanese court elite would have considered polygyny (but not polyandry)[502] an acceptable form of marriage. West also says in a general discussion of Javanese culture that 'the nobility and prijaji [bureaucrats] have traditionally practiced polygyny, the ability to have more than one wife, while most other Javanese have not done so'.[503]
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ present ♥ From her study of the Old Javanese kakawin epics, a genre which existed from the 9th to 15th centuries CE, Creese reconstructs a world in which 'victors redistribute women of defeated enemies as spoils of war who subsequently become concubines at the courts'.[504]
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ unknown ♥ I have not come across any information which would allow us to estimate the typical number of extramarital partners with whom elites produced offspring in this period.

Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ present ♥ We have evidence that distinctions were made between men and women in some areas of Old Javanese law. For instance 'Most regulations [in the Old Javanese/Balinese law codes] protected the ownership rights of a husband or master over a woman. Compensation was paid to the husband, master or guardian of the woman in much the same way as reparation was required for the loss of other goods or livestock.'[505]
♠ Social class ♣ present ♥ Creese notes that the Old Javanese law codes - which incorporate influence from 'Sanskrit thought and culture' as well as pre-Islamic Javanese social mores - list 'different levels of fines or punishments according to the rank of the offender'.[506] These law codes were in use 'from at least the twelfth century in Java'.[507]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ inferred present ♥ It has been difficult to find evidence of how different ethnic groups were treated under Old Javanese law, but the outcome of the case recorded in the Wurudu Kidul jayapattra of 907 CE (from the earlier polity of Medang) suggests that being a member of an established wanua (irrigated rice-farming community) conferred special legal rights - 'Only a member of the community (anak wanua) enjoyed full rights in the kěraton society.'[508][509] Writing on precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, Scott notes that '[e]cological niche, because it marks off different subsistence routines, rituals, and material culture, is one distinction around which ethnogenesis can occur'.[510] In an ancient Javanese context, this could have meant separate ethnic identities for rice-cultivating anak wanua and swidden cultivators living at higher altitudes, but I have not found concrete evidence for this.

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions) In 1540, Pinto explained that the rulers of Java had always been accustomed to treat of the most important matters of their state by mediation of women, especially where it concerns peace, as God has given more gentleness, courtesy, and authority to women than to men, who are severe and thus as a consequence less agreeable to those who are sent. Women frequently appear as negotiators on early Javanese inscriptions.[511]

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ present ♥ Creese comments that van Setten van der Meer's study of Javanese agrarian society from the 5th to 15th centuries CE shows 'that women held various official positions'.[512]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to both males and females ♣ inferred present ♥ Javanese inscriptions from the 10th century, slightly before this period, demonstrate that women 'could either inherit or acquire titles in their own right, and there is evidence that they took a prominent part in village decision-making'.[513] As de Casparis has shown, such titles were accompanied by administrative responsibilities,[514] so the fact that women could 'acquire' them could be seen as a kind of promotion within a loosely defined bureaucracy. I have not come across any statement to the effect that this situation changed under Airlangga or the Kediri kings, so have (tentatively) coded 'inferred present' for this variable.
♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ present ♥ In the Sumanasāntaka, a courtly Javanese narrative poem composed around 1204 CE,[515] the Princess Indumatī is considered old enough 'to study the sacred texts with a great sage' after she loses her first milk teeth.[516] The text is a Javanized version of a Sanskrit epic and its characters belong to an 'aestheticized court culture',[517] but this at least signals that education was considered desirable for noble girls and women in the Kediri kingdom. Supomo also describes how from at least the early 10th century, Sanskrit literary culture was spreading from the kraton into rural areas. 'The earliest evidence of the penetration of the Sanskrit epics into rural areas is found in the Sangsang copper plate inscription issued in the name of King Balitung in AD 907 to confirm the granting of freehold to the monastery of Dalinan ... To celebrate what must have been the most important event of the year, if not of decades, for the whole region, a variety of performances was given on that occasion ... the inscription also mentions a wayang performance (mawayang) of the story of "Bhīmaya-kumara", and a recital (macarita) of the "Bhīma-kumara" and "Rāmāyaṇa" stories. Both "Kicaka" [a type of dance mentioned in the inscription] and the "Bhīma-kumara" obviously refer to a certain episode from the Wirā taparwa, the fourth book of the Mahābhārata ... Since there is no evidence that a Javanese translation or adaptation of the Mahābhārata already existed at that time, it seems likely that the narrator of the Bhīma-kumara episode recited a Sanskrit text, and then, as in a harikatha [a public reading of the Sanskrit epics in India], explained it to his audience in Javanese.'[518] We know from the sima inscriptions that both men and women were present at ceremonies such as this, so I have coded present in recognition of the didactic function of literary performance for ordinary Javanese villagers after the 10th century.
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ present ♥ The inscription of Jayapattra Diedukman, from 907 CE, 'records the case of the debt of a woman who passed away ... The creditor wanted to recover the debt from her husband but the latter did not know about his wife's debt and, according to Old Javanese law, he should not have been a defendant in this case because the debt concerned his wife's property'.[519] Though this inscription comes from the earlier Medang Kingdom, I have seen no evidence to suggest that this protection of women's property under Old Javanese law changed in the Kahuripan or Kediri periods. More generally, Creese comments that van Setten van der Meer's study of Javanese agrarian society from the 5th to 15th centuries CE shows that women were able 'to inherit and to own land, and to dispose of property'.[520]

Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent ♥ In a discussion of epithets used to refer to members of the ancient Javanese village, Wisseman Christie says that 'The most common epithet, si, apears to have been applied to village members in general, but was not usually given to members of the state hierarchy above the village level, except for a few at the lowest levels of the bureaucracy'.[521] This seems to imply that bureaucratic position was a function of one's rank in society.
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent ♥ In a discussion of epithets used to refer to members of the ancient Javanese village, Wisseman Christie says that 'The most common epithet, si, apears to have been applied to village members in general, but was not usually given to members of the state hierarchy above the village level, except for a few at the lowest levels of the bureaucracy'.[522] This seems to imply that bureaucratic position was a function of one's rank in society.
♠ Education is available to people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ Supomo describes how from at least the early 10th century, Sanskrit literary culture was spreading from the kraton into rural areas. 'The earliest evidence of the penetration of the Sanskrit epics into rural areas is found in the Sangsang copper plate inscription issued in the name of King Balitung in AD 907 to confirm the granting of freehold to the monastery of Dalinan ... To celebrate what must have been the most important event of the year, if not of decades, for the whole region, a variety of performances was given on that occasion ... the inscription also mentions a wayang performance (mawayang) of the story of "Bhīmaya-kumara", and a recital (macarita) of the "Bhīma-kumara" and "Rāmāyaṇa" stories. Both "Kicaka" [a type of dance mentioned in the inscription] and the "Bhīma-kumara" obviously refer to a certain episode from the Wirā taparwa, the fourth book of the Mahābhārata ... Since there is no evidence that a Javanese translation or adaptation of the Mahābhārata already existed at that time, it seems likely that the narrator of the Bhīma-kumara episode recited a Sanskrit text, and then, as in a harikatha [a public reading of the Sanskrit epics in India], explained it to his audience in Javanese.'[523] We know from the sima inscriptions that a wide variety of people from different status categories were present at the ceremonies, so I have coded present in recognition of the didactic function of literary performance for ordinary Javanese villagers after the 10th century.
♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ present ♥ 'Some form of individual ownership of land appears to have been both widespread and common to all strata, with the bulk of the productive land held by the villagers who worked it'.[524]

Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ present ♥ During the 'era of east Javanese hegemony', foreign port-based merchants could be 'employed as royal tax collectors in areas near their port of residence'.[525] Hall thinks that the use of foreign tax collectors at this time might indicate political instability and an 'attempt by the Javanese monarch to penetrate local political autonomy'.[526]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ present ♥ Focusing on the Central Javanese period, John Miksic told us that some foreigners in Java were given tax farms and became 'collectors of the king's due'. Some of them probably married into the royal family.[527] This implies that certain foreigners, those who were favoured by the king, could receive official titles and administrative responsibilities (and be 'promoted' as the king saw fit). My general impression is that there were no rigid laws about eligibility for various bureaucratic roles: titles, and their attendant administrative responsibilities, could be granted by the king (or a rakai) to anyone who had in some way earned his favour, and we know that foreigners were also employed as tax-collectors in the East Javanese period.[528]
♠ Education is available to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ present ♥ In a discussion of the history of writing in Southeast Asia, Reid says that 'In earlier stages of the spread of writing at Buddhist hands, the barriers were low to sharing or accepting that writing across the still-embryonic boundaries between civilized and barbarian, believers and pagans'.[529] A 'Buddhist monkhood that knew few boundaries ... spread its habits of scholarship also to forest retreats and hilltop sacred places'.[530] In Reid's view, it was only later (after around 1400 CE) when, under the influence of 'Confucian, Islamic, and Christian notions of civilization', the barriers between the inhabitants of the valley-dwelling states and the stateless periphery became 'substantial'. For the latter, writing then 'became associated with both alien religion and alien state power',[531] but in this earlier period I think we can assume that 'ethnic' identity did not determine one's access to education.
♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ inferred absent ♥ John Miksic told us that foreigners in this period were restricted to certain areas, and although they were sometimes given the right to collect taxes, there is no evidence that they owned land.[532] Another indication that there may have been restrictions on property ownership by those who did not belong to a certain wanua community is de Casparis' statement that 'A Java, la terre appartenait en général à celui qui la cultivait mais avec des restrictions. Le cultivateur ne pouvait pas, par exemple, vendre ses terres à des personnes qui ne résidaient pas dans le même village. Toutes les exceptions à cette règle devaient être approuvées par le conseil du village, car il était de l'intérêt commun que les terres ne passent pas à des étrangers'.[533] (In Java, the land generally belonged to those who cultivated it, but with some restrictions. The cultivator could not, for example, sell his land to people who did not live in the same village. All exceptions to this rule had to be approved by the village council, as it was in the common interest that the land did not pass to strangers.)

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ Wisseman Christie refers to 'aristocratic' families and the 'upper classes' in Java from the early 9th century onwards, implying a system of inherited high status.[534] Carter Bentley has written that across early Southeast Asia, 'membership in social strata was often hereditary, either in principle or in effect, though status ascription took a bewildering variety of forms'.[535]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ present ♥ Dellios writes of the Hindu-influenced polities of mainland and island Southeast Asia: 'Another unrealised dimension of the Hindu world was that of caste: Southeast Asian societies did not privilege lineage or social position as much as "persons of prowess" ... An individual's achievements - not birth status - were considered indicators of spiritual power.'[536] There are even hints of achieved power in the concepts surrounding Old Javanese kingship: Dellios has argued that the ruler him- or herself 'represents the charismatic centre of a mandala and is considered a person of "prowess"'. [537] In other words, 'Hindu-Buddhist traditions of kingship, introduced into Southeast Asia by the seventh century A.D., saw the monarch as a repository of karma, or merit'.[538] To speak more specifically of Kediri-period Java, Wisseman Christie describes an evolving situation in Java after about 1000 CE in which merchants and landowners could use their wealth to climb up the social ladder. She discerns the reaction of the state against this process in the appearance of sumptuary regulations in the inscriptions of the time: 'Tax farming became a lucrative source of income for merchants with extra cash to invest, and trade wealth was transmuted into status through the establishment of private family temples by the nouveau riche, who invested money in creating sima foundations for them. One sign of the destabilization of the status system under the impact of new wealth was the appearance in inscriptions of sumptuary regulations. During the eleventh century, in east Javanese inscriptions rather less space was given to market details as rulers became more preoccupied with fine-tuning sumptuary restrictions'.[539]
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ present ♥ Carter Bentley has written that across early Southeast Asia, 'membership in social strata was often hereditary, either in principle or in effect, though status ascription took a bewildering variety of forms'.[540] Sumptuary restrictions in 11th-century east Javanese inscriptions show a system of hereditary status being reasserted (according to Wisseman Christie) against the threat of newly wealthy merchants: 'One sign of the destabilization of the status system under the impact of new wealth was the appearance in inscriptions of sumptuary regulations. During the eleventh century, in east Javanese inscriptions rather less space was given to market details as rulers became more preoccupied with fine-tuning sumptuary restrictions'.[541]

♠ social mobility ♣ present ♥ Although the caste system had some currency in ancient Java, in comparison with India the system was less rigid.[542] Wisseman Christie describes an evolving situation in Java after about 1000 CE in which merchants and landowners could use their wealth to climb up the social ladder. She discerns the reaction of the state against this process in the appearance of sumptuary regulations in the inscriptions of the time: 'Tax farming became a lucrative source of income for merchants with extra cash to invest, and trade wealth was transmuted into status through the establishment of private family temples by the nouveau riche, who invested money in creating sima foundations for them. One sign of the destabilization of the status system under the impact of new wealth was the appearance in inscriptions of sumptuary regulations. During the eleventh century, in east Javanese inscriptions rather less space was given to market details as rulers became more preoccupied with fine-tuning sumptuary restrictions'.[543]

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ equal ♥ Wisseman Christie tells us that 'The ruler, during this period [9th-13th centuries], was also a rakai [member of the landed gentry] and maintained direct rights over his watĕk even after he has ascended the paramount throne. He was normally drawn from amongst the small group of rakais holding direct rights over very large watĕks, or groups of villages from which tax income could be drawn ... With a few exceptions, subsequent generations of rulers appear to have been attached to those old watĕks in Central Java which formed the core of the original state'.[544]
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ present ♥ According to the Pararaton, the Javanese 'Book of Kings', the last king of Kediri, Kertajaya, was dethroned by a former criminal. 'Ken Angrok, a man of low origin, murdered the Regent of Tumapel and usurped his place by marrying his widow Ken Dedes. He then availed himself of a quarrel between Kertajaya and his clergy to attack Kediri. In 1222 he defeated the king at the battle of Ganter. Then as King Rajasa he built his kraton at Kutaraja, later known as Singosari'.[545] Although the Pararaton cannot be accepted as a straightforward, historically accurate account, this does seem to indicate than a commoner rose up to dethrone Kertajaya in a violent coup.
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ ♥ JR: I have not found evidence of named high-status individuals moving into low-status groups in the Kediri Kingdom, but it seems possible: we know that debt was a 'major route to servitude' in this period,[546] and that 'indebtedness was a common condition even for the elite'.[547]

♠ occupational mobility ♣ present ♥ Wisseman Christie has referred to the bakul market trader who was active in Java by 900 CE or earlier as an 'entrepreneur', implying that these pedlars could choose their occupations.[548] Using the evidence from sima charters of different varieties of coloured and patterned cloth and their association with social status, she has also outlined 'profound shifts' in Javanese social structure during the 11th and 12th centuries CE, occurring 'against a background of rapid population growth, increased trade wealth and overseas contact for a broader spectrum of the Javanese populace'. In her view, these shifts entailed an increase in 'social fluidity' as older village structures broke down.[549] Moreover, the content of the lists of professional taxable commercial activities in the sima charters changed considerably over time: the number of professional traders listed increased as time went on.[550] To me, her characterizations strongly suggest some degree of occupational mobility in Java from at least 1000 CE.

Slavery[edit]

Bas-reliefs depict a hierarchised society including slaves. [551]. There has been much discussion about the status of slavery. We need to consider that temple 'slavery' could be something of an honour or a purely ritual slavery although those who toiled on temple land could also be seized captives. De Casparis and Mabbett refer to literature specifically on Cambodia to substantiate this but I see no reason why the same could not be said of Java given the religious parallels. [552]

We asked John Miksic about slavery and bonded labour in the ancient Javanese kingdoms, and he explained that 'there were obviously different levels of servitude [...] People captured in war were the worst off, because then they could be forced, they were under some compulsion, we can say that, but then other people sought to attach themselves to a noble ... we have evidence only for the 14th century for this, we don't have evidence earlier on, but it seems like most of the settlement patterns of this period were like a nobleman and his court and then a bunch of their adherents living around them for protection, because there is a lot of banditry, we can be pretty sure of this based on later sources, there's a lot of banditry [...] there were roving bands of robbers and you needed some nobleman to protect you from that. And, villages were probably fortified [...] so there were levels of servitude and it's all very indistinct in linguistic terms [...] so, you know, "bondsmen", "royal bondsmen", yes, there were such things but most of it was voluntary'.[553]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ unknown ♥ I have not found any source of evidence that would allow us to determine what proportion of the population was enslaved, and the uncertain tone of Wisseman Christie's discussion suggests that we cannot give a solid figure for this at this point: 'Slavery was apparently known, at least in East Java, and some village members had slaves or servants within their households. While some of these may have been debtors or contractual servants, the number of people carried off in slave raids from the north coast of Bali indicates that a regular slave market may have existed in East Java by early in the second millennium, if not before'.[554]

♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ 0 ♥ 'Royal bondsmen and professional artisan groups provided the continuous labor' for the construction of temple complexes in central Java in the earlier Medang period.[555] However, Hall doesn't really define 'bondsmen' here, and it appears that there was no equivalent in ancient Java of the medieval European serf, bound to a certain parcel of land. Miksic explained that noblemen and officials had to attract people to settle down and work the land: 'they couldn't compel you to stay'.[556]

Types

♠ Chattel slavery ♣ present ♥ Wisseman Christie has written on the subject of credit and debt in 9th- to 12th-century Java (a time of 'commerce' and 'monetization'), and notes that debt was a 'major route to servitude'.[557] 'Debt-bonded individuals could be pawned by their masters, and under certain conditions also sold outright, although they retained in principle the right to "redeem their bodies" by buying themselves out of bondage. It is conventional wisdom that forms of bondage were less severe, and slavery as such less common, in the major states of Southeast Asia than in its stateless peripheries ... Christie's observations, however, show that in medieval times, at least, this contrast was not marked, slavery and debt bondage being in both settings nearly indistinguishable'.[558] Those in debt-bondage could find sanctuary by fleeing to the tax-grant (sima) communities where they were 'protected from seizure by their masters'.[559] 'Apparently too the state, albeit acting indirectly through the medium of religious institutions, was in some degree concerned to limit the degradation of its free subjects, via debt, to private chattels'.[560] However, I have coded present here because alongside the institution of debt-slavery, Wisseman Christie describes the development of other, perhaps less fluid forms of bondage by around 1000 CE: 'Slavery was apparently known, at least in East Java, and some village members had slaves or servants within their households. While some of these may have been debtors or contractual servants, the number of people carried off in slave raids from the north coast of Bali indicates that a regular slave market may have existed in East Java by early in the second millennium, if not before'.[561]
♠ Elite slavery ♣ present ♥ From her study of the Old Javanese kakawin epics, a genre which existed from the 9th to 15th centuries CE, Creese reconstructs a world in which 'victors redistribute women of defeated enemies as spoils of war who subsequently become concubines at the courts'.[562]
♠ Bonded serfdom ♣ inferred absent ♥ 'Royal bondsmen and professional artisan groups provided the continuous labor' for the construction of temple complexes in central Java in the earlier Medang period.[563] However, Hall doesn't really define 'bondsmen' here, and it appears that there was no equivalent in ancient Java of the medieval European serf, bound to a certain parcel of land. Miksic explained that noblemen and officials had to attract people to settle down and work the land: 'they couldn't compel you to stay'.[564]
♠ Corvée labor to a landowner ♣ present ♥ Geertz claimed that the corvée labour owed to the king in early Southeast Asian states 'was but the king's share in the village labor pool, in which the chief, various other local officials, and the mass of the villagers themselves also had certain rights'.[565]
♠ Corvée labor to the state ♣ present ♥ In early Southeast Asian states, 'the primary responsibility of the peasant to the monarch was corvée.[566]

Restraints

♠ Owners can execute slaves ♣ ♥
♠ Owners can impose corporal punishment on slaves ♣ ♥
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ present ♥ The Agama, an Old Javanese law code, 'regards a fair number of crimes as punishable by death'.[567] The Kutara Manawa law code stipulates that if a man has sex with a female member of the royal household, a crime known as 'stepping on the (royal) pillow', he should be put to death.[568] Boomgaard comments more generally that 'Since prisons were, as a rule, a European introduction in Southeast Asia, it is not surprising that pre-modern Southeast Asian law relied on other sanctions: fines, compensation, and corporal punishment, with the death penalty as the extreme example'.[569]

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Some form of human sacrifice took place in Java in the Medang kingdom, before this period. Discussing human skeletons found at several temple complexes in central Java, in some cases from deep shafts beneath cult statues, Jordaan and Wessing say that 'the possibility of the practice of human sacrifice in ancient central Java' can 'no longer be ignored'.[570] Hall has also commented that 'sacrifices of animals and even human beings were common to Javanese Tantric ritual as depicted in the tenth-century text Sanghyang Kamahāyānikan.'[571] Tantric Buddhism had reached Java by the 7th or 8th century,[572] but other (older) interpretations have seen these killings in the light of Javanese folk tradition. Stutterheim made reference to 'the Javanese culik belief, which involves a belief in human sacrifice in connection with large construction projects'.[573] Unfortunately - perhaps because the practice of human sacrifice does not sit well with established ideas about Hinduism and Buddhism in ancient Java - the human remains from Prambanan and elsewhere have been neglected (for instance, I do not know of any osteological analysis that would allow the victims' sex or age to be determined) and for this reason we know very little about the status of the victims. I have coded absent/present in curly brackets to indicate that there was a tradition of human sacrifice, associated with the Tantrism that continued to be prevalent in the East Javanese kingdoms of Kahuripan and Kediri. However, its extent and form is unknown, and some scholars (including an anonymous referee of Jordaan and Wessing 1996) have questioned the evidence.[574]

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred present ♥ Kings of the Singhasari and Majapahit eras were considered incarnations of gods. [595]

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ present ♥ The court encouraged the elite of its rural communities to think themselves as belonging to the king's hierarchical, kin-based order which necessitated the king imagining that he could control powerful supernatural forces associated with ancestors. [596]

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ present ♥ Kings of the Singhasari and Majapahit eras were considered incarnations of gods. [597]

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ present ♥ Kings were thought to reunite with the god from whom they were incarnated. [598]

♠ Automatic deification ♣ present ♥ Kings were thought to reunite with the god from whom they were incarnated. [599]

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ present ♥ Kings of the Singhasari and Majapahit eras were considered incarnations of gods. [600]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ absent ♥ Kings of the Singhasari and Majapahit eras were considered incarnations of gods. [601]. However, it is worth noting that one of the key differences between South Asian and Javanese Hinduism is that the latter lacked a true caste system [602].

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ Kings of the Singhasari and Majapahit eras were considered incarnations of gods. [603].
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ One of the key differences between South Asian and Javanese Hinduism is that the latter lacked a true caste system [604]. DM: While Javanese Hinduism did not have a true caste system, it did distinguish between political elites and commoners.
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ absent ♥ People from India and Sri Lanka were singled out for praise because they came from the land of Buddha or Siva or Vishnu. No specific groups were depicted as inferior [605].

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ absent ♥ Polygamy was allowed [614].

♠ Ideological thought reinforces prosociality ♣ present ♥ Generosity was one of the key moral values in this polity [615]. The following may also be relevant, though it relates to Hinduism more broadly rather than any specific Javanese variant: "According to Hindu Dharmashastras, of the four stages of life, the person who is at the householder stage is the anchor of society. The householder’s business is the maintenance and support of those in the other three stages of life: the students who have not yet entered into the world of work, the retirees who have seen the birth of grand- children and spotted their first gray hairs, and the renunciants, those who have left behind not only the world of work and material possession but who have also left behind family and self-interest for a life focused on spiritual freedom. Householders are the ones with material wealth, and their responsibility, their dharma, is to share it with others. [...] The link between the world of the householder and the world of the renunciant is gift giving—dana. The classic image is of the renouncer with the begging bowl, making the rounds for alms. On a daily basis, in Hindu society, food is given to sannyasins and sadhus, the world renouncers who have 'cast off' from the settled world of 'this shore" [616]

♠ charity ♣ present ♥ Generosity was one of the key moral values in this polity [617]. The following may also be relevant, though it relates to Hinduism more broadly rather than any specific Javanese variant: "According to Hindu Dharmashastras, of the four stages of life, the person who is at the householder stage is the anchor of society. The householder’s business is the maintenance and support of those in the other three stages of life: the students who have not yet entered into the world of work, the retirees who have seen the birth of grand- children and spotted their first gray hairs, and the renunciants, those who have left behind not only the world of work and material possession but who have also left behind family and self-interest for a life focused on spiritual freedom. Householders are the ones with material wealth, and their responsibility, their dharma, is to share it with others. [...] The link between the world of the householder and the world of the renunciant is gift giving—dana. The classic image is of the renouncer with the begging bowl, making the rounds for alms. On a daily basis, in Hindu society, food is given to sannyasins and sadhus, the world renouncers who have 'cast off' from the settled world of 'this shore " [618]
♠ production of public goods ♣ inferred present ♥ Generosity was one of the key moral values in this polity [619].
♠ communal dining ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ morality ♣ present ♥ "Universal dharma relates to all humanity and it includes ethical virtues such as compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence. These virtues that are said to be common to all humans are known as sadhārana (common) or sanatana (eternal) dharma. [...] Elsewhere Manu (VI:91- 2) asserts that avoiding backbiting, hypocrisy, greed, infatuation, anger and discontent is approved dharma for all the stages of life. The problem is that there are different views as to what the actuality of universal dharma is. Radhakrishnan, for example, argues for a sanatana dharma, based upon Advaita Vedanta thought, that is said to be present in all religious traditions. It is a kind of perennial philosophy that is at the heart of every religious tradition not just at the heart of the Hindu tradition. Others concentrate upon a universal dharma within the Hindu tradition itself but there is no absolute consensus as to what it is. Nevertheless, in theory, the ethical virtues of compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence are universally regarded by most Hindus." [620]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ absent ♥ It was a common belief that to be a slave would be the result of one’s karma in a previous life, so it would be due to evils perpetrated in a previous incarnation [621]. The following may also be relevant, though it relates to Hinduism more broadly rather than any specific Javanese variant: "The roots of slavery stretched back to the earliest Hindu texts, and belief in reincarnation led to the interpretation of slavery as retribution for bad deeds in an earlier life. Servile status originated chiefly from capture in war, birth to a bondwoman, sale of self and children, debt, or judicial procedures." [622]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ absent ♥ It was a common belief that to be a slave would be the result of one’s karma in a previous life, so it would be due to evils perpetrated in a previous incarnation [623]. The following may also be relevant, though it relates to Hinduism more broadly rather than any specific Javanese variant: "The roots of slavery stretched back to the earliest Hindu texts, and belief in reincarnation led to the interpretation of slavery as retribution for bad deeds in an earlier life. Servile status originated chiefly from capture in war, birth to a bondwoman, sale of self and children, debt, or judicial procedures." [624]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery universally ♣ absent ♥ It was a common belief that to be a slave would be the result of one’s karma in a previous life, so it would be due to evils perpetrated in a previous incarnation [625]. The following may also be relevant, though it relates to Hinduism more broadly rather than any specific Javanese variant: "The roots of slavery stretched back to the earliest Hindu texts, and belief in reincarnation led to the interpretation of slavery as retribution for bad deeds in an earlier life. Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic ♥ "According to one popular formula, there are 330 million gods in Hinduism. Nevertheless, most Hindus have believed that there is ultimately one deity, and that the diversity of other gods are his or her emanations or manifestations, representing his or her agency in particular contexts. [...] The three gods most commonly worshipped as the ultimate in Hindu traditions are Visnu, Siva, and the Goddess, variously called Devi, "Goddess," and Sakti, "Power." This triad of deities must be distinguished from an overlapping group of three deities who are conceived in the Puranas and other authoritative traditions to perform particular cosmic functions-Brahma, who creates the universe anew in each vast cosmic cycle, and also, in some accounts, bestows the Veda upon humanity; Visnu, who preserves the universe by intervening to rectify problems through his avataras, "descents" or "incarnations"; and Siva, who destroys the universe at the end of each cosmic cycle. [...] [W]hichever deity is conceived as the ultimate is understood to be the source who emanates the others, both the triad who perform the aforementioned cosmic functions and lower supernatural beings, and thus to be the ultimate agent in their actions. [...] There are numerous other important deities besides those frequently conceived as a monotheistic ultimate. One of the most important of these is Ganesa, a son of Siva with the head of an elephant, believed to be a protective figure that removes obstacles. Another is Hanuman, the monkey-god who helped Rama in his battle against Ravana, idealized for his devotion to Rama and Sita. Other deities important in particular regions include the South Indian Ayappa, regarded as the son of Siva and a female avatara of Visnu; and smallpox goddesses such as the North Indian Sitala." [646]
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ [present; absent] ♥ The gods of Javanese versions of Hinduism were depicted as having quite human traits, just like the Greek or Roman gods. Buddha and other Mahayana Buddhist deities however were all-seeing and all-knowing [647].
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ [present; absent] ♥ The gods of Javanese versions of Hinduism were depicted as having quite human traits, just like the Greek or Roman gods. Buddha and other Mahayana Buddhist deities however were all-seeing and all-knowing [648].
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ inactive ♥ "Each Purana tends to focus on a specific deity as the Supreme Being responsible for creating and ordering the world. [...] Thus, [for example,] Krishna [...] is a form of the supreme God who creates, maintains, and destroys the universe. In the Bhagavad Gita , Arjuna is permitted to glimpse Krishna's cosmic form. He sees the entire universe, the sun, stars, and planets, and all the people gathered on the battlefield within the body of Krishna. Krishna tells him that everything that exists is part of himself, but that he is not limited to everything that exists. In other words, just as the Vedic Purusha extended ten finger-lengths beyond the created universe, so too Krishna is the entire cosmos and more." [649] "Among the cosmic laws that constitute dharma, the law of karma, or action, is especially relevant to rebirth. By the law of karma, every thought, word, and deed carries with it a like reaction. Beings are born, live, die, and are reborn based on their actions. Every good deed brings a reward, and every bad deed a punishment. The terms 'reward' and 'punishment' are deceptive, however; for karma is not seen as a set of rewards and punishments doled out by a just God, but in impersonal terms, as the working of natural law. Karma attracts reactions to moral agents like an ethical law of gravity. Benevolent actions produce good results for the person who engages in them. Similarly, actions intended to cause suffering will lead to suffering for those who commit them." [650]

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code "compound" moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ present ♥ "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one's activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one's actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [651] "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one's cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [652]

[656] "Universal dharma relates to all humanity and it includes ethical virtues such as compassion, generosity, gratitude, and non-violence. These virtues that are said to be common to all humans are known as sadharana (common) or sanatana (eternal) dharma. For example, the Law of Manu (X:63) argues that non-injury, truth, not stealing, purity and control of the senses summarise dharma for the four castes." [657]
♠ supernatural enforcement of kinship obligations ♣ present ♥ "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one's activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." [658] "As a religiously informed orientation to life's actions and obligations, dharma gives guidelines to how husbands, wives, children, and parents should conduct themselves. Many of the selections from sources included here advise their audiences on the roles proper for various situations and consider some of the complexities and ambiguities that arise in living according to their discipline. [...] Deeply embedded in Hindu notions of marriage is a disposition toward protection. It is the husband's obligation to protect his wife from external predators and temptations and to invest in a relationship that will sustain her commitment to him and his world. At the same time, it is the obligation of the wife to protect her husband by feeding, caring for him, and investing in a relationship that will sustain his commitment." [659]
♠ supernatural enforcement of in-group loyalty ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ supernatural enforcement of respect ♣ present ♥ "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one's activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one's actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [660] "The Brahmins, although 'priests', were higher in the caste hierarchy than the Kshatriyas, the 'rulers'. Not all Brahmins have been priests and not all Kshatriyas have been rulers. The complexity of the caste system, regional and linguistic differences and historical change have all militated against this rigidity. [...]Brahmins have traditionally been seen as ritually more pure than Kshatriyas and more versed in religious knowledge. They are, in theory if not always in fact, the key scholars in the Hindu tradition. The Kshatriyas have traditionally been seen, again in theory if not always in practice, as the rulers who maintain society, uphold dharma and protect the householder system. They built and sponsored temples, protected their subjects and their deities and, to that extent, the Brahmins recognised a ruler's overlordship. However, the kingly role in ruling and guardianship duties involved more 'polluting tendencies' than were usual in a Brahmin's life. The Brahmins have had a dual role in society. Those who are active priests in temples have engaged in functions that involve being given gifts by worshippers and such temple priests are more involved with lower castes. Paradoxically Brahmins who are versed in promoting scholarship and in advancing religious learning but who do not act as serving priests are, in general, more widely admired because they have less contact with the public. Thus Brahmins who are not priests can be more akin to those renouncers engaged in lifelong learning and spirituality, rather than in the hurly-burly of householder life. To that extent they would receive greater respect than Kshatriyas." [661]
♠ supernatural enforcement of bravery ♣ present ♥ "Early Jainism was prepared to countenance the exercise of martial skills in war but only if carried out defensively, and it denied the possibility, frequently stated in Hinduism, that a warrior who died in battle would be reborn in heaven (Bh 7.9)."[662]
♠ supernatural enforcement of purity ♣ present ♥ "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one's activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one's actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [663] "The strength of the caste system has been enhanced by another principle emphasised by Dumont (1980) in his classical work entitled Homo Hierarchicus: The Caste System and its Implications. According to Dumont, in addition to the distinctions already outlined that fit varnas and jatis into hierarchies, another must be added. This distinction takes caste hierarchical divides beyond the kind of class distinctions found in eighteenth-century Britain or in the Ancien Regime in Europe, by stressing the opposition between purity and pollution. Caste difference, according to this classification, goes beyond class differences into this other realm of ritual purity and pollution. The Brahmins are the highest and purest varna, and the Dalits are the lowest, in that they are outside varna and are impure altogether. The other varnas rank in between. Pollution occurs in different ways. It occurs through bodily contact of one sort or another, with menstrual cycles, through birth and death, through emissions such as faeces, urine and saliva, and through contact with night-soil, dirty clothes, unswept rooms and so on. Thus marriage and sex have to function within the correct set of jatis in the correct varna. Polluting jobs such as laundering and sweeping are done by people of lower castes so that the blood of people of higher castes can remain 'pure'. Equally menstrual activity among older women makes them more 'impure' than men or pre-menstrual virgins, and therefore women are more polluting than men. They will thus often live semi-separately while menstruating, during which time they will also avoid going to the temple and therefore 'polluting' the gods. Furthermore, untouchable Dalits were prohibited from entering many temples before the post-Independence freedom-of-temple-entry legislation and even now they are kept out of some temples in many villages. [...] It is also true to say that Dumont's thesis suffers from exaggeration and other matters are important in discussing caste. Nevertheless, there is at least an element of truth in the notion that purity and pollution has relevance in any discussion of caste." [664]
♠ general supernatural enforcement of morality ♣ present ♥ "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one's activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one's actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [665] "Coming from the Sanskrit root word Dhar (sustain), Dharma has been interpreted variously as a religious code of behavior, a divine system of morality and righteousness, and conformation to one's cosmically allotted duty and nature (Radhakrishnan, 1968). It is a comprehensive term, incorporating benevolence to humanity, moral restraint (yama), and observance of duty or obligation (niyama). Yama required the active avoidance of violence (ahimsa), dishonesty, falsehood, jealousy, avarice, and other vices." [666]

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ inferred absent ♥ The following quote shows that caring for others is an important value in Hinduism: "According to Hindu Dharmashastras, of the four stages of life, the person who is at the householder stage is the anchor of society. The householder’s business is the maintenance and support of those in the other three stages of life: the students who have not yet entered into the world of work, the retirees who have seen the birth of grandchildren and spotted their first gray hairs, and the renunciants, those who have left behind not only the world of work and material possession but who have also left behind family and self-interest for a life focused on spiritual freedom. Householders are the ones with material wealth, and their responsibility, their dharma, is to share it with others. [...] The link between the world of the householder and the world of the renunciant is gift giving - dana. The classic image is of the renouncer with the begging bowl, making the rounds for alms. On a daily basis, in Hindu society, food is given to sannyasins and sadhus, the world renouncers who have "cast off" from the settled world of "this shore."[667] However, no evidence could be found for actual enforcement of this behaviour--e.g. punishment for not giving food to renouncers. Instead, it seems any punishment or reward was believed to occur through reinarnation: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [668]
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to Hindu Law: "Misconduct by doctors, judges and examiners of coins has been transferred from steya to sahasa by Mādhava who has prescribed the highest punishment for a quack, a judge who punishes an innocent person and lets the guilty go scot free, and for an examiner of coins who lies about the genuineness of coins. These rules from Narada are available in all the texts under review, but Varadaraja and Devana have placed them under steya. One may conclude that while Varadaraja and Devaṇa saw dishonesty by these professional as mere financial bungling, Mṇdhava took their misconduct more seriously." [669] However, it seems that Hindu Law had only a nominal link to Hindu precepts, and does not therefore count as "human enforcement of morality": "The growth of law in India may be likened to the evolution of disciplines like grammar, poetics, etymology, and prosody etc. Early speculations on these started in the samhitās and gained strength as auxiliaries to the Vedas. Soon, however, these sciences freed themselves from sacerdotal concerns and came to be established as independent subjects of enquiry, paying only lip service to the authority of the Vedas. The same may be said to be true of law which was an appendage to sacred literature only in the period of the sūtras. Even earlier than the earliest of the known metrical smṛtis, the artha śāstra tradition had recognised that politics and administration were outside the domain of the sacred." [670]

Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [671]

♠ respect for property ♣ inferred absent ♥ Referring to commentaries on Hindu law between the eighth and fourteenth centuries CE: "The law on theft in the period under review seems to have been already well settled much earlier. Texts of this period exhibit near unanimity in their presentations. [...] Some of the subjects highlighted in these texts are: definition of theft, types of theft, punishments for various types of theft, collaborators, special laws of evidence to prove theft, liability of the state to compensate for theft and so on and so forth."[678] However, it seems that Hindu Law had only a nominal link to Hindu precepts, and does not therefore count as "human enforcement of morality": "The growth of law in India may be likened to the evolution of disciplines like grammar, poetics, etymology, and prosody etc. Early speculations on these started in the samhitās and gained strength as auxiliaries to the Vedas.
