♠ Moralistic punishment by human religious agents ♣ absent ♥ This variable is meant is to reflect whether or not religious agents had the authority to punish moral transgressions independently of the state. In this polity, religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [681].

Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):

♠ physical punishment ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [682].
♠ exile ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [683].
♠ loss/seizure of property or wealth ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [684].
♠ loss of status ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [685].

Extent of punishment (can be more than one):

♠ punishment of rulers ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [686].
♠ punishment of elites ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [687].
♠ punishment of commoners ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [688].

Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ ♥
♠ Price controls ♣ ♥
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ ♥
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ ♥
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ present ♥ 'Several surviving original texts [from 9th- to 12th-century Java and Bali] do record the clearance of existing debts. In these cases the people concerned are of high social status, and the sums of money considerable. The debts in question, moreover, are long-standing and often inherited, suggesting that indebtedness was a common condition even for the elite'.[689]
♠ Pensions ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention in the sources of any pension provision.
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ present ♥ 'In the corpus of surviving original - rather than later reissued - Old Javanese language inscriptions, the earliest mention of a system of weights and values is dated 843 A.D., and it comes from the state of Mataram.'[690] By 860 CE, 'a uniform system of monetary weight-values had already been recognized by the court' and was used for expressing the value of 'taxes and tithes, the value of labour and services, interest and repayment schedules on debt, and transactions involving commodities ranging from land and buildings down to domestic food purchases'.[691] The Mananjung inscription of 928 CE provides evidence for the involvement of the east Javan kings in regulating trade, including a royal instruction on 'the use of standards and approved weights and measures at warehouses and rice granaries'.[692][693]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ ♥
♠ Public markets ♣ present ♥ Already by the 8th and 9th centuries in central Java, there was a 'hierarchical marketing network' based at the simplest level on periodic village markets known as pken or pkan.[694] '[T]he pken markets provided the forum for the watak's [village cluster's] external commercial contacts, the place where "foreign" merchants were allowed to trade, and thus facilitated the flow of local production upward to supralocal market centers as well as receiving in return the downward flow of foreign goods for local consumption from the supralocal markets.[695]

Contracts

♠ State enforcement ♣ present ♥ In a discussion of the 'regulation of marriage and sexuality' in the Old Javanese and Balinese law codes, Creese notes that 'Marriage contracts were ... treated like any other form of contract in the Old Javanese law codes with set penalties for breaches if the "goods" were not delivered or were faulty or deficient in some way.'[696] The codes acted as a practical legal manual 'from at least the twelfth century in Java' and Creese treats them as instruments of the early state in the Indonesian archipelago.[697]
♠ Non-state enforcement ♣ ♥
♠ Corporate Agency ♣ ♥
♠ State contracting ♣ ♥
♠ Limited Liability ♣ ♥

Patronage

♠ within-class formal patronage ♣ ♥
♠ between-class formal patronage ♣ present ♥ Helen Creese has described how the 'Indic courts' of pre-Islamic Java functioned as 'major centres of patronage of the arts', including of court poets who composed kakawin (literary epics) between the 9th and 15th centuries.[698] We know the names of several poets and their royal patrons in this period (for instance, Hari's Lineage was written by Mpu Panuluh under the patronage of King Jayabhaya), suggesting that this was a formalized relationship of court patronage.[699]
♠ within-class informal patronage ♣ ♥

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ inferred absent ♥ Speaking generally about wet rice-cultivating (sawah) areas in early southeast Asia, Hall says that during famines, the population would resort to eating taro, yams and sago palms, which were normally disdained: 'Rice might be affected by periodic disease and plagues; mice plagues are shown in Borobudur (Java) and Angkor (Cambodia) temple relief. But fish (from rivers, tanks, or sawah fields) and coconut (when properly maintained) were virtually free of pests and diseases. Rice was eaten by preference to other grains or other kinds of starches. Reliance upon other staples was looked down upon except during rice famines when sawah cultivators could normally turn to tubers (taro, which grew in sawah areas) and yams (which were gathered from nearby forests or were grown in the rain-fed fields) as well as to sago palms, which were another stable source of starch.'[700] Takács and Cline comment that 'Early Asian rice cultivators also supplemented their diet by networking with highland hunters and gatherers, both to reduce the highlanders' inclination to raid their villages and to exchange their diverse agricultural produce for forest products (such as woods, bamboo and lacquer) and meat'.[701] I have not come across any evidence of the deliberate provision of famine relief by any state, religious or non-state agents, and Wisseman-Christie says 'there is no evidence that [9th-century Javanese villagers] maintained communal granaries'.[702]

♠ provision of famine relief ♣ ♥ As far as I can tell, the rice-farming population would simply adopt different subsistence strategies in times of famine, turning to taro, yams and sago and trading more with 'highland hunters and gatherers'.[703] I have not come across evidence for public storehouses of rice or other staples set aside for famine relief.

♠ Food storage ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sedyawati refers to granaries in the rajya, the 'city of the king',[704] but does not specify who had access to these. I have found no evidence of food storage facilities intended for use by a broader community rather than for the exclusive use of their owners. Wisseman Christie thinks that 'Although the early Javanese markets were undoubtedly more limited in scope and flexibility than their modern counterparts, they apparently served not only the consumption needs, but also some of the storage and credit needs of the farmers'.[705] Her implication is that this was mediated by 'professional rice vendor[s]'.[706]

♠ provision of food storage ♣ ♥

♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ inferred absent ♥ Speaking generally about wet rice-cultivating (sawah) areas in early southeast Asia, Hall says that during famines, the population would resort to eating taro, yams and sago palms, which were normally disdained: 'Rice might be affected by periodic disease and plagues; mice plagues are shown in Borobudur (Java) and Angkor (Cambodia) temple relief. But fish (from rivers, tanks, or sawah fields) and coconut (when properly maintained) were virtually free of pests and diseases. Rice was eaten by preference to other grains or other kinds of starches. Reliance upon other staples was looked down upon except during rice famines when sawah cultivators could normally turn to tubers (taro, which grew in sawah areas) and yams (which were gathered from nearby forests or were grown in the rain-fed fields) as well as to sago palms, which were another stable source of starch.'[707] Takács and Cline comment that 'Early Asian rice cultivators also supplemented their diet by networking with highland hunters and gatherers, both to reduce the highlanders' inclination to raid their villages and to exchange their diverse agricultural produce for forest products (such as woods, bamboo and lacquer) and meat'.[708] I have not come across any evidence of alimentary supplementation as a public good provided by some state, non-state or religious body: as far as I can tell, the rice-farming population had several techniques at their disposal (such as trade with forest hunter gatherers) to supplement their diet.

♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ ♥

♠ Drinking water ♣ inferred absent ♥ Miksic believes that 'the unique problems connected with maintaining healthy drinking water supplies in a tropical environment help to explain why early urbanization in Indonesia ... is relatively rare'.[709] We do not have solid archaeological evidence for urban sites in central or east Java until the 14th-century (Majapahit) site of Trowulan, which features canals and reservoirs that may have served both symbolic and practical functions.[710] If we follow his line of argument, we might infer that the Javanese before the 14th century had no need for public drinking water distribution systems (irrigation is another matter) because they were not clustered in dense settlements; or, conversely, that the early Javanese did not live in dense settlements because the problems of providing clean drinking water in tropical urban settings had not yet been solved. Either way, I think we can infer an absence of systems for providing drinking water to the general population.

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ ♥

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ 'Initiatives in the Kadiri state formation also included the development of a system of water management (supposedly for both transportation and irrigation purposes).'[711]

♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ mixed ♥ The Kediri state certainly played a role in irrigation provision: it is at this point that a 'special government official' called the senapati sarwwajala appears, with responsibilities related to the system of water management.[712] However, it seems that as in earlier periods, irrigation was also locally managed. Wisseman Christie says of community officials with duties related to local water management, for which we have evidence from Medang-era inscriptions, that 'there is no reason to doubt that [they] continued to fulfil similar roles until at least the fifteenth century. Community water officials were chosen from amongst the land-holding heads of household belonging to the community council'.[713]

♠ Health infrastructure ♣ ♥

♠ Control of infectious disease ♣ inferred present ♥ It is clear that in the preceding period, the Kahuripan Kingdom, controlling infectious disease and protecting the population through ritual means was considered one of the main duties of the monarch. The Old Javanese text of the Pucangan Stele of 1041 CE says that Airlangga 'had to acquire the dignity of his forbears by gaining rajalaksmi and wahyu keraton for restoring world peace, renewal of religious services, and removal of the plagues of the world'.[714] Likewise, in early Bali, which shared many aspects of its Hindu-Buddhist culture with neighbouring Java, Lovric states more explicitly that 'a causal relationship is assumed between human welfare on the one hand, and the proper enactment of rituals, the care of temples, devotion to the gods, and propitiation of the vast array of demonic beings on the other. It was the duty of the ruling raja to stage large-scale rituals considered crucial to the well-being of subjects and the survival of the kingdom'.[715] It seems likely that control of infectious disease through supernatural power was also a feature of Kediri kingship.
♠ Care of war invalids/veterans ♣ ♥
♠ Care of orphans ♣ ♥
♠ Care of elderly ♣ ♥

♠ State pensions for elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention of state pension provision in any of the Hindu-Buddhist Javanese kingdoms.

♠ State pensions for non-elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention of state pension provision in any of the Hindu-Buddhist Javanese kingdoms.

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ ♥

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Theatres ♣ inferred absent ♥ Although we have evidence of public wayang (Javanese puppet theatre) performances and dances as early as the 10th century (during the Medang period),[716] I have not come across any mention in the sources of buildings set aside for theatre in the Kediri Kingdom.
♠ Arenas ♣ ♥
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ Many ritual buildings. Hall argued that 'the many chandis scattered about East Java celebrating a dead ruler in the guise of Śiva, Vishnu or the Bodhisattva Avalokitesvara were all centres of ancestor-worship and, although outwardly Hindu or Buddhist, represented a cultus that was a survival from the pre-Hindu past'.[717]
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ accessibility of entertainment buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ present ♥ In a discussion of the structure of the Javanese state from the 9th to 13th centuries, Wisseman Christie lists 'magicians ... actors, musicians, and dancers'. One of their sources of support seems to have been tax-collecting rights bestowed by the king.[718]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ ♥

♠ Access to education ♣ ♥ The Brahmanic doctrine prescribed contemplation in forest hermitages for the higher strata of society after the completion of worldly duties. These asramas developed into centres of education. These became self-sufficient communities and pupils had to complete manual labour in lieu of paying dees. Similar institutions called mandalas emerged in the last century of Majapahit and are described in the Tantu Panggelaran. [719]

♠ Publicly funded or subsidized education ♣ ♥
♠ Schools for children? ♣ ♥
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ ♥
♠ Libraries ♣ present ♥ Creese refers to kakawin literary works being preserved in 'royal libraries' and copied and recopied over centuries.[720] Kakawin were composed in Java between the 9th and 15th centuries.[721]
♠ Museums ♣ ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ rare ♥ In his book on statelessness in Southeast Asia, Scott argues that 'literacy in premodern societies was, under the best of circumstances, confined to a minuscule portion of the population, almost certainly less than 1 percent ... To assert, in this context, that a whole society or people is literate is incorrect; in all premodern societies the vast majority of the population was illiterate and lived in an oral culture, inflected though it was by texts'.[722]

Biological Well-Being[edit]

Mortality

♠ Life expectancy ♣ unknown ♥ John Miksic told us that although we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms, scholars suspect that it was quite good.[723] Because we lack evidence either from skeletal analyses or from any kind of record-keeping on births and deaths, I think we cannot come up with a figure for average life expectancy at this time. The long-term annual rate of population growth in premodern Indonesia is generally believed to have been very low, hardly exceeding 0.1 % per annum up to 1750. According to Anthony Reid "constant low-level warfare" led to high deaths in pre-modern Indonesia - the mobilization of large armies and the taking of large numbers of captives sold into slavery. [724]
♠ Infant mortality ♣ unknown ♥ John Miksic told us that although we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms, scholars suspect that it was quite good.[725] Because we lack evidence either from skeletal analyses or from any kind of record-keeping on births and deaths, I think we cannot come up with a figure for infant mortality at this time. David Henley believes the absence of weak and unhealthy adults observed by European travellers suggests high rates of infant mortality as only the strong could survive regular food shortages. [726]

Disease

♠ Endemic disease ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Different sources give varying impressions of prevalence of disease in the ancient Javanese kingdoms. Scott writes that the 'demography and agro-ecology of state space ... makes it more vulnerable to instability in food supply and to illness ... we have ample evidence of crop failures, famines, and cholera outbreaks in precolonial Southeast Asia. Although somewhat speculative, it is likely that the concentration of rice and men in state spaces carried their own substantial risks'.[727] His comments are oriented toward the 'padi states' of precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, but may be considered relevant to the irrigated rice cultivators in Hindu-Buddhist Javanese states. Miksic comments that 'Irrigation can ... be associated with water-borne diseases. A particularly important example is urinary schistosomiasis, which is spread by contact with water where there live snails which form the intermediate host. A study in Madagascar found that urinary schistosomiasis spread rapidly to new areas along with the expansion of an irrigation system ... It is not known whether such problems occurred in ancient Java, but one must remember that the expansion of the irrigation system may well have caused new health problems while eliminating others'.[728] However, Miksic also told us that although we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms, scholars suspect that it was quite good.[729] He believes that the rarity and late appearance of cities in Indonesia may have been a way of avoiding the higher disease burden and other 'dangers and inconveniences' associated with urban settlement patterns.[730]
♠ Disease outbreaks ♣ inferred present ♥ Lovric comments that 'Considering Java's geographical position on the trade-route between India and China and the centuries-old two-way traffic, it seems unlikely that the island could have been by-passed by pandemics of the plague until the twentieth century'.[731] Sustained maritime trade between India and Southeast Asia can be dated to the 'later centuries of the pre-Christian era'.[732]
♠ Endemic malnutrition ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Different sources give varying impressions of the diet of the average subject of the ancient Javanese kingdoms. Scott writes that the 'demography and agro-ecology of state space ... makes it more vulnerable to instability in food supply and to illness ... we know that the grain diet of early agrarian societies was nutritionally inferior to the mixed diet it replaced; and finally, we have ample evidence of crop failures, famines, and cholera outbreaks in precolonial Southeast Asia. Although somewhat speculative, it is likely that the concentration of rice and men in state spaces carried their own substantial risks'.[733] His comments are oriented toward the 'padi states' of precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, but may be considered relevant to the irrigated rice cultivators in Hindu-Buddhist Javanese states. On the other hand, Jákl thinks we can infer from the descriptions of food in the kakawin epics that the peasant diet was relatively rich and varied. He comments: 'It may be wrong to extrapolate modern dietary patterns of Javanese peasants, with their heavy reliance on rice and vegetables, back on the conditions of pre-Islamic Java. In relatively sparsely populated premodern Java, with abundant resources and few documented dietary taboos, peasants may have generally enjoyed a much more varied diet then their modern descendants. It seems that predominantly, or purely, vegetarian dietary regimes were a matter of a conscious choice rather than a necessity'.[734]

Physiology

♠ Average height (male) ♣ ♥ Reports from early European travelers often emphasised the tall stature and good health of the local population which Reid believes indicates favourable living conditions. [735] Barrow reports that the Javanese in 1793 were "the middle size of Europeans". [736]
♠ Average height (female) ♣ ♥ Reports from early European travelers often emphasised the tall stature and good health of the local population which Reid believes indicates favourable living conditions. [737] Barrow reports that the Javanese in 1793 were "the middle size of Europeans". [738]

Prosocial Behavior[edit]

♠ Volunteering ♣ ♥

♠ Charity ♣ ♥

Voluntary Associations

♠ voluntary trade associations ♣ ♥
♠ prestige of voluntary trade associations ♣ ♥
♠ voluntary community organizations ♣ [absent; present] ♥ By the 9th century CE, Javanese villages featured a governing council: 'the karāmān - whose membership was large, running at times into the hundreds'. However, from Wisseman Christie's description it appears that membership was not entirely voluntary. The karāmān 'appears to have comprised all core or full-membership, adult, married villagers, their status probably dependent upon a combination of land rights and position as head of household'.[739]
♠ prestige of voluntary community organizations ♣ ♥
♠ voluntary athletic clubs ♣ ♥
♠ prestige of voluntary athletic clubs organizations ♣ ♥
♠ other voluntary organizations ♣ ♥
♠ prestige of other voluntary organizations ♣ ♥
♠ voluntary religious associations ♣ present ♥ De Casparis and Mabbett describe how a 'true alternative society' of ascetics and monks devoted to 'an archaic form of Brahmanic Hinduism' flourished on the Dieng plateau in central Java 'from early times to at least the eleventh century'.[740] On the basis of later texts such as the Tantu Panggelaran (c. 1500), it seems likely that voluntary religious associations continued to exist in Java past 1100 CE. This text 'describes numerous hermitages and communities of ascetics especially in the mountain areas. When parts of the lowlands had already been Islamized, Brahmanic culture still survived in the mountainous areas of eastern Java for a considerable time.'[741] More generally, maritime Southeast Asia was influenced by 'an important feature of the Indian way of life': 'the possibility to opt out of ordinary society to choose a life of contemplation - either as a hermit in the forest or as a member of some religious community'.[742]
♠ prestige of voluntary religious associations ♣ equal ♥ The Brahmanic áśramas found in early Java were probably very prestigious, as they attracted the 'higher classes' and were perceived as places where one could acquire 'wisdom' and teach others.[743] We can infer from religious iconography that ascetic gurus enjoyed very high status in Javanese thought. For instance, the niche usually occupied in South Indian Śiva temples by 'Śiva Dakșiņāmūrti, the "Supreme Guru"' is replaced in Java by a 'representation of the sage Agastya. It may seem strange that a ŗși [an ascetic or court brahmin], however wise and powerful he may have been, should be represented on a par with deities like Durgā and Ganesa, but it is known that Agastya, for whom a temple and an image were made in east Java in 760, enjoyed special veneration in Java. Whichever is the correct interpretation, there is no doubt that the Guru was one of the most popular representations in ancient Java. This may well reflect the particular awe felt for a teacher in Java till recent times'.[744] For this reason, I have coded 'equal': those who joined these communities seem to have been equal in status to the top nobility.

♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ ♥

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ one sixth ♥ of a subject's income - the only figure I have found suggested for ancient Javanese taxation, but this is by no means a secure estimate. De Casparis describes how ancient Indian theories of statecraft prescribed that this portion of income should go to the state, but we have no way of knowing whether it also applied in ancient Java: 'La théorie indienne prescrit le chiffre d'un "sixième" des revenus comme norme, mais il n'y a aucun moyen de savoir si, ou jusqu'à quel point, on s'y tenait en Indonésie'.[745]

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ Wisseman Christie refers to '[a]gricultural taxes' during the 9th to 13th centuries in Java.[746]
♠ Property ♣ present ♥ There is, however, a change in focus from taxation on larger communities to smaller subunits. [747]
♠ People ♣ ♥
♠ Trade ♣ present ♥ cattle, pottery, salt and oil. [748] List of taxable goods traded in Javanese rural markets includes tin from Malaya, copper from China and mainland Southeast Asia, iron from sources perhaps as distance as the East African coast, silver perhaps from Burma, and gold from many sources. [749] Sedyawati says that in the Kediri kingdom, 'Taxes were collected on different undertakings, such as the sale of cattle, of metalworks, of earthenwares, of provisions like salt and oil...'[750] Hall highlights the evidence from inscriptions that 'rulers of east Java's states received specified shares of ... goods supplied regularly by domestic traders, such as spices, ceramics, and cloth of foreign origin.'[751]
♠ Income ♣ present ♥ In specific cases, income may have been taxed - Sedyawati says that the Kediri state taxed 'services, like performances'.[752] Presumably part of the performer's fee would have been paid to tax collectors.
♠ Production ♣ present ♥ metalwork [753] We also have evidence from inscriptions that 'rulers of east Java's states received specified shares of local products such as rice and cloth'.[754]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ present ♥ 'Inscriptions report that rulers of east Java's states received specified shares of local products such as rice and cloth, as well as goods supplied regularly by domestic traders, such as spices, ceramics, and cloth of foreign origin'.[755]
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥ Rakai, local lords, could extract taxes from the population of the particular watĕk village group over which they exercised authority.[756]
♠ state officials ♣ present ♥ During the 'era of east Javanese hegemony', foreign port-based merchants could be 'employed as royal tax collectors in areas near their port of residence'.[757] Hall thinks that the use of foreign tax collectors at this time might indicate political instability and an 'attempt by the Javanese monarch to penetrate local political autonomy'.[758]

Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ Rice.[759] 'Central Java states ... expected both taxes in kind and labor service from their subjects. Inscriptions report that rulers of east Java's states received specified shares of local products such as rice and cloth, as well as goods supplied regularly by domestic traders, such as spices, ceramics, and cloth of foreign origin'.[760]
♠ Labor ♣ present ♥ 'The pre-1500 political systems, whether wet-rice or riverine, commonly attempted to draw the resources of their realms - in the form of tribute, talent, men, and goods - to their centers. Central Java states, for instance, expected both taxes in kind and labor service from their subjects.'[761]
♠ Currency ♣ present ♥ In a paragraph discussing tax and trade in Java from the 10th century onwards, Wisseman Christie states clearly: 'Gold and silver coins were minted in both central and east Java, and they were used by all strata within the society to pay taxes and debts'.[762]

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥ I have not found a figure for the annual salary of a labourer for the Medang, Kahuripan or Kediri Kingdoms, but I suspect that this may be due to lack of access to some key volumes of collected inscriptions. The information may be available in de Casparis (1950, 1956).[763] Wisseman Christie comments that 'The contents of the surviving inscriptions of ninth and early tenth century Java suggest that ... the monetary price of commodities and labour ... remained rather fluid for some time after the introduction of coinage',[764] which implies that inscriptions are already giving information on the price of labour from the 9th century onwards.

♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ ♥ I have not found a figure for the staple cost of rice (or any other carbohydrate) during the Kediri Kingdom, but I suspect that this may be due to lack of access to some key volumes of collected inscriptions. The information may be available in de Casparis (1950, 1956).[765] Wisseman Christie comments that 'The contents of the surviving inscriptions of ninth and early tenth century Java suggest that ... the monetary price of commodities and labour ... remained rather fluid for some time after the introduction of coinage',[766] which implies that inscriptions are already giving information on the price of commodities from the 9th century onwards.

♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥ Hall describes wealthy members of the court elite in the 10th-13th centuries as well as newly rich families, especially after the 'economic/market boom' during the reign of Airlangga (Kahuripan Kingdom). 'Javanese consumerism in the tenth through thirteenth century era included kraton members as well as the socially ambitious.'[767] 'As one historian characterizes this age: "Public grandeur [i.e., the construction of lavish temple complexes] was, for a time, displaced by private luxury"'.[768]

♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ ♥ New forms of wealth and power based on commerce seem to have emerged in Java around the turn of the millennium: Hall describes wealthy members of the court elite in the 10th-13th centuries as well as newly rich families. 'Javanese consumerism in the tenth through thirteenth century era included kraton members as well as the socially ambitious.'[769] 'As one historian characterizes this age: "Public grandeur [i.e., the construction of lavish temple complexes] was, for a time, displaced by private luxury"'.[770]

Kinney, A.R., Worshiping Siva and Buddha: The Temple Art of East Java, Honolulu: University of Hawaii Press, 2003.

Reid, A., Southeast Asia in the Age of Commerce, 1450-1680, Volume 1: The Lands Below the Winds, New Haven, CT: Yale University Press, 1988.

Ricklefs, M.C., A History of Modern Indonesia, ca. 1300 to the present (second edition), London & Basingstoke: Macmillan; Bloomington, IN, Indiana University Press, 1993.

Schoppert, P. et al, Java Style, Indonesia: Periplus Editions, 1997.

Sedwayati, E., 'Airlangga', in Ooi, K.G., Southeast Asia: A Historical Encyclopedia, from Angkor Wat to East Timor, Santa Barbara, CA: ABC-Clio, 2004 (a).

Sedwayati, E., 'Kediri', in Ooi, K.G., Southeast Asia: A Historical Encyclopedia, from Angkor Wat to East Timor, Santa Barbara, CA: ABC-Clio, 2004 (b).

Sedwayati, E., 'Singhasari', in Ooi, K.G., Southeast Asia: A Historical Encyclopedia, from Angkor Wat to East Timor, Santa Barbara, CA: ABC-Clio, 2004 (c).

Sinha, P.C., Encyclopaedia of South East and Far East Asia, Volume 5, Delhi: Anmol Publications, 2006.
